II. DUPOUK-FjiiEOFGB wanted to add that the architects were not library exoerts.

Mi 3TCEPANI raised the question of possible extensions tf the Socretariat during the next three years. How would it to possible to get roc rooms for new developments?
M. HAAS had the same apprehensions as M. Stoppani.
He thought that the present buildings, including the new house, were sufficient for the present year, but would be insufficient if there were new developments after January 1st 1929 .
Marquis Pii.ULUCCX said the Supervisory Commission did not share this opinion, and thought that the right procedure, if there were new developments, would be to ash the Assembly for new housing corresponding to the new developments.

6.	M. RGEfDGIO said that the Political Section was going on with the work of preparation for the Security Committee.
M. Rutgers, who was entrusted with the question of the Articles of the Covenant, was at Geneva, and M. Holsti, who was entrusted with the arbitration question, would arrive on Saturday.
M. Politis was not coming to Geneva.

contact which he had successfully begun in Rome. He had obtained
satisfactory results in Italy, as regards both the question of the
relations between the league and the Italian Government and the
questions to he dealt with by the new Arbitration and Security Commit tee .

The &3CB5Tj^Y~GI31in3RAl said it was proposed to place on the Agenda the ouestion of the diplomatic privileges of Judges of the Permanent Court of International Justice at The Hague* This was a serious question. He understood that two or three of the Judges were prepared to resign if the prepent position were maintained and that others would ask fcr the removal of the Court frore The Hague. Ha would suggest that, as soon as the Agenda had been distributed, the President of the Council or the Rapporteur invited to deal with the subject should write to the President of the Court and to the Fatherlands representative on the Council, expressing the hone that the Question would 'only come before the Council in the form of an agreed settlement between the parties,and suggesting that the parties should negotiate with that' object in view. He understood that both parties ware prepared to negotiate, but that neither of there felt inclined £c taka the first step. The intervention of the President of the Council or the Rapporteur might accordingly be useful.
Ha understood that the Judges of the Court would be satisfied if they received from the Fetherlands Government the same traatmant as the higher officials of tha Sacretariat- ox the league of Rations received from the Swiss Government.

G3H5EAL DISCUSSION.
The SSCRSTARY^SiSRAL described and briefly commented uuon the draft reports for the meeting to be held at Prague between the President and the three reporters of the Committee on Arbitration and Security. The representatives had met under the chairmanship of M, Benas. The report of M. Eolsti on arbitration and conciliation contained valuable suggestions,
He would refer especially to a proposal put forward by M. Giraud in a draft general treaty for conciliation, a clause of which provided that the Council should net be deprived of its powers to intervene for the speedy settlement of a dispute, even though conciliation orocedure had been started to deal with it. The second report, by M. Rutgers, was a commentary on certain Articles of the Covenant and raised in its original form a number of rather difficult questions which would have to be further considered.
The third report, by M. Politis, dealt with the question of security. Reference was made in the renort to regional pacts, under which the various parties might agree to accept a decision of the Council
5
passed by a majority of to e-thirde if a dispute should arise under Article 15; though no objection could bo taken tc States making such an arrangement, it ecu Id not of course bind the Council to take a decision by a two-thirds majority or t o apply Article 16 as a result of such a decision. It was further proposed that all such pacts should be submitted tc the Council for approval before registration, and M. Politis indicated that it might be desirable for the Council t* examine any existing treaties so as to ascertain whether they ware in conformity with the Covenant. The objection to this last proposal was that provision was already made in the Covenant as regards treaties witch might not bo compatible with it, and that to introduce other provisions for that purpose would tend to throw a doubt on the validity of the existing stipulations.
Sir Arthur SALTER announced that the complicated financial negotiations with the Creek Soveminent were novs on the point of being successfully concluded. The Creek Loan would be issued at a rate of interest l/ less than that of the previous loan.
M. ACEUILES said that the Polish and Lithuanian Governments had expressed themselves as ready to sond represent a tives either to Warsaw or to Riga to negotiate. The questions •A the Agenda included circulation on the frontier, postal and telegraphic communications, railway communications and various transit questions. The Lithuanian Government, in expressing its readiness to negotiate, had emphasised the need of carefully preparing the ground and had complained that the persons expelled from Vilna in November had- not yet been allowed to return. M. Vo Idem ares had asked the French Minister at Warsaw
6
to intervene, but the French Minister had refused.
M. Voldemar as hod proposed that the Conference between the two Governments should deal with the subject of compensation for damages in Vi In a as a result of its occupation by Zsligcwski. M. Voldemar as had said that ho was pro oared to go to the proposed Conference in tier son.
The S.3CT ETAEY-GiDKSRAX said that he had urged upon M. ookal the importance of allowing the persons expelled from Vilna to return.
M. RCBTIKG said that, as a result of the recent elections, a Coalition Government had been constituted at Danzig , which was proposing to proceed to a revision of the Constitution. The Council might accordingly ha called upon to consider several important constitutional questions in the near future.
The meeting rose at 5-15 p.m.

Le Comité examine la note et les propositions de M. Buxton, préalablement approuvées par le Secrétaire général ( Director*s Meetingj Paper 5. 31 février 1920- ] .
Le SECRETAIRE ŒEEERAL remarque que la quantité de documents distribués par le Secrétariat menace do démunir intolérable . Il a en vue par exemple le cas typique cité dans la note de M. Buxttm relativement au Sous-Comité de la protection de l’enfanoe, qui doit se réunir le 19 mars, et pour lequel il a déjà été distribué 550 pages ronéographiées et 170 pages Imprimées. H convient, d’ailleurs, que le cas est particulièrement grave pour la Section sociale .
Quelles sont le3 me sures à prendre pour remédier à cet
état de choses ? II.
s
b ©Table quo la première mesura à recommander serait qu'au lieu
de s'embarquer dans 1' étude 3bune disaine de questions dif-
enj
fêrentes, chaque comité se borne à/étudier deux, Ces deux une fois complètement étudiées, on pourrait alors pa.s&er à d ou ic c u o s t i 0 ns no u ve L1 es
En fait 3 il suffit ou: une organisation particulière puissante obtienne qu'une question soit placée à l'ordre du jour pour ru’aussi tôt le temps et l’argent de la Société soient mis immédiatement à contribution et pour eu1une masse de documents voie le jour,
Il revient	sur la	réforme	énergique qui s	'impose,	N a
s p. vo ir, qu e c ha ou e	00mi t	é dresse	un programme de	travail	li
mité à un petit nombre de questions pour chaque année, et n'entreprenne rien de nouveau avant que ce programme soit épuisé.
Par ailleurs, ne serait - il pas possible que les l'encres de Sections résument telles réponses à des questionnaires lorsqu'elles sont très longues ? Il lui apparaît que ce devrait être parfaitement possible, excent ion faite, cependant, pour las questions de droit, Les documents juridiques sont généralement trop techniques et de nature trop délicate pour se prêter à pareil résumé.
H, À7E1TOL estime que c'est vraiment sut Secrétariat ou'il appartient de limiter les matériaux de travail à soumettre aux Comités. Un Comité no travaille jamais par lui-même. Ce sont seulement les membres du Secrétariat ou les rapporteurs qui étudient les collections documentaires cotaplè-
tes, Les Membres individuels du Comité ne peuvent lire tous les documents ot, en fait, ne les lisent pas. H. Aveno 1 fait
observer, au surplus, qu'il y a lieu de distinguer entre les documents ronéographiés et les documents imprimés.
3
Les premiers sont généralement des documents courts: les seconds sont, au contraire, en principe, plus longs. Il va de soi que bon nombre de documents sont nécessaires pour les 3-ouvernements. A l’origine des critiques relatives à cette surabondance de documents imprimés, se trouvent les divers Ministères des Affaires étrangères, chargés de distribuer les documents aux services intéressés. Ces critiques trouvent leur écho dans les services des Gouvernements en rapport avec la Société des dations.
Il lui apparaît que toute cette question de _a surabondance des publications de la Société des hâtions n’est pas seule ment une question de mesure, mais presque de sécurité - du moins en ce qui concerne les crédits budgétaires demandés à l’Assemblée pour ces diverses impressions.
LS SECRETAIRE 0-SH3HAL estime que c’est surtout aux secré
r
taires des comités qu'incombe la responsabilité de veiller à ce que la quantité de documents ronéographiés ou imprimés soit réduite. Et à cet effet, il insiste à nouveau sur la nécessité qui s'impose d’amener les Comités à limiter le nombre des questions étudiées par eux.
Le Dr. HALL ne croit pas impossible qu'une amélioration puisse être apportée, en ce qui concerne les documents préparatoires des Comités. Il convient qu’en ce qui concerne le Comité de la Protection de l’enfance, les Membres ont, en effet, une énorme quantité de documents à digérer. Il fait,toutefois,observer que la plupart du temps le Secrétariat ne fait qu'agir en vertu d’instructions. Il est chargé de recueillir des matériaux, lesquels doivent naturellement être transmis.

BUDGET Or TEE LDAGUL, BOR 1929.
The SECRETARY-GENERAL said that the question of the Budget would he tahen nest week, when he hoped the various Directors would he available for the discussion of their estimates. The Supervisory Commission would meet on April 25th.
The Seeretary-General said that while he anticipated increases in the Budget for 1929 on the Economic side, and also as regards Opium - because of the constitution of the new Opium Board - and Transit, he very earnestly hoped that increases for other Sections would be very small, as he foresaw that there would be very great difficulties if a largely increased Budget was submitted to the next Assembly.
PI ¿¿US ¿ION Oh TIE LAST ¿CUEOIL MEETING.
The SECABTiM.Y-GM~L.AAL said that according to press comments there was a general feeling that the work of the last meeting of the Council was open to considerable criticism. • He did not think that such criticism had the sane importance as previously, as the League could stand it now.
2
He had "been .very much struck by a speech by jx i Stre semann to the effect that public opinion must not be led to expect that because the Council was meeting and important questions were submitted to it it could automatically solve these questions, The great value of discussion by the Council was to dissipate the acute feeling that had grown no on specific questions; to extract, so to speak, the poison from a que st ion.
There was another conclusion which might he drawn from the last Council meeting; it was clear that when there was a question of a draft that had not been seen by the Council, the latter should adjourn, in order that a text might b e ade qu at e ly pre p are d.
CONFERENCE OK SKINS AND BONES.
The SECRSTARY-GENSRAH understood that it was likely that on the questions before the Conference European countries at least wculd come to an agreement. He was very anxious that in any agreement which might be reached there should he a formula which wculd provide for possible revision if conditions in non-European countries rendered such revision necessary. He suggested that M. Teixidor, who had great experience in these questions should attend the Conference as well as Dr. Barandon and should consult with M. Berruys as to what form the necessary revision should take.
ARRANGEMENTS FOR THE CPU MCI! ROOM.
M. COMERT thought there would he a great improvement if the Council room was arranged in such a way as to permit the members of the Council to withdraw to a private room when necessary.
- 6 -
Ha wanted, moreover; to lay stress on the suggestion that the proposed arrangements for the Council room should aim
'4
at giving a more imposing character ts the meeting. He suggested, firstly, that the Connell platform should he higher, and secondly, that there should he better order in the out lie .

marquis PAULUCCI DI CALBCH BARG HE remarked that although the police were responsible for maintaining order in any individual cases, in the extent of any general manifestation, such as applause etc., it was the duty of the President of the Council to request the public to maintain silence .

THE PORTUGUESE LOAN.
Sir ARTHUR SALTER said he wanted to dispel misapprehensions about this question. On the political side,
the Council, acting through the Committee of the Council, had

- 4 -
given the necessary authorisation to proceed with the work.
There remained the cuestión of the legal aspect, and the question of the so, ci al character of tha control. As regards the legal position the Portuguese Government must either state that it had retained the old eonst itu tion, and would act according to its requirements, or that there was a new constitution, with different requirements for the issue of a loan. As regards the control, negotiations had not teen broken off. The Portuguese representative had wished to refer to his Government. In view of the criticisms it v;as essential to make it clear that the loan contemplated was not a loan to a Government, but a loan to and for a country, to be used for specified objects certified as in the interest of the country and made available under conditions assuring that it was used only for these objects. Among the safeguards required for this purpose the Financial Committee con-
siderec	1 it essential that	there should	be	an officer from	the
Do ague	r e s id e nt in Li sb on	. Moreover,	as	the representati	ves
of the old re'gima had said that they would repudiate the loan if they returned to power the Financial Committee thought it necessary to provide that in case of such default a commission similar to the one which had been created in Greece should be constituted. It was on these two points that the Portuguese Minister desired to refer to his Government. There had been no difference cn the technical proposals.

2. PESSo EXHIBITION AT PCLOG-KB.
The SBCRSfARY-GSKERAL said there would open at Cologne on May 1st a large and very important exhibition of matters concerning the press« The question arose whether or not the league should participate in the exhibition. Three methods of participati&n had Lean suggested:-
1)	that a member of the Secretariat should be
present at the opening meeting;
2)	that lecturers should be sent to Cologne from
time to time ;
3} that there should be a permanent exhibition, especially of pamphlets and publications of special interest to the press.
The Sec ret ary-General agreed that the first and second
proposals were advisable . As regards the third he
thought that with one exception the Secretariat had never
exhibited at an international exhibition. In this particular
case the Secretariat might be accused of propaganda mating
as it might be suggested that the connection between the
League ana the press was not very close. Moreover, if the
present invitation was accepted it might be difficult to
refuse subsequent invitations.
On the other hand it was true that the League could La held to be in a peculiar position as regards the press, and acceptance of the invitation would not make it necessary to participate in any other kind of exhibitions. It was possible in the present case to avoid
- 3
being accused of making propaganda« A large number of Governments was exhibiting. The League, which was formed of Governments, was accordingly entitled to exhibit.
While it could be held that in this particular case participation by the League would be justified, each further case should be considered on its merits«
Dr* DORMAN WE 123 referred to precedents in the case of the Health Section. The League had been invited to exhibit at the annual meeting of the Medical Association«
It had been invited another time in Spain, and last year at Dusseldorf. Diagrams, charts and samples of literature had been sent. This had been found useful and had led to enquiries as to the work of the Xeague. He thought it was almost one’s duty to exhibit.
M. DUFOUR -? EEC LC E agreed with the Secretary-General.
M. COLBAN said it was a question of measure. If M. Gomert felt sure "that raxticipati on in the exhibition would not put the Secretariat in a position of disadvantage in comparison with other participants, he agreed the Secretariat ought to exhibit .
Mr. HIAM said he had represented the Secretariat at an exhibition at Bale. One of the difficulties was that the League might get a second prize.
The S3 CAE TAR Y-GENERAL said that it was clear that when the League participated it should be hors concours.
- 4
3 • BUJJG^T a
The SSCRSTi&f-CENEBAX said he had been going through the Budget for next ye arc The amount ox the increase was not two millions, as had teen feared; it was one million two hundred thousand. Ho wanted to draw attention first to the fact that there were requests for 38 new posts, out of a total of 647, and secondly to the fact that there was a very great number of international conferences proposed for 1929 , namely, five for economic and financial questions, two for
*
social questions, two for disarmament questions (the General hisarmament Conference and the Conference on the private manufacture of arms), one for the codification of international law* Out of these nine c inferences three ox four were of the first importance, and it was clear that no international staff could ccpe with so many conferences in a year. As these conferences had "been decided upon by the Assembly it must be for the Assembly to say which of them would be postponed until 1930. The Secretary-General intended to point out to the Supervisory Committee that ha was bound to budget for all these conferences and leave the final decision to the Assembly itself.

1.	BUDGET OF TEE LEAGUE FOR 1929 .
The SECRETARY-GENERAL said that he had keen to Paris with Marquis Paulucoi di Calboli Barone for the meeting of the building Committee. He had spoken to M. Osusky about the Budget. M. Osusky had agreed that it was necessary to set out definitely before the Assembly the financial results of the policy which it had decided to follow. It was for the Assembly to take the responsibility for postponing a certain number of the international conferences contemplated for 1929.
2.	BRAZIL ARB THE LEAGUE.
The SECRETARY-GENERAL had had a conversation with a
well informed Brazilian, who had told him that the next fortnight
would be the oritioal time. The Council7s appeal would arrive .
at Rio in the course of the next three or four days, and
consultations would then take place. The ex-President was very
-2-
strongly against tbs return of Brazil to the League, and he was supported in this by the ex-Minister for Foreign Affairs.
The present Minister for Foreign Affairs was favourable to Brazil's re-entry and it Was therefore necessary to give him such su; support as could be found. The opponents to the return of Brazil to the League said that the idea of a demarche by the Council had originated with the South-American States represented on the Council and was not supported by European powers. So it would be well if three or four of the smaller European Powers not members of the Counoil sent a special appeal to the Brazilian Government. It had. also been suggested that some leading artioles might be put in the press. The Secretary-General had been able to oarry out these suggestions in a certain measure and he felt that as far as the Secretariat was concerned everything that could be done had been done.
3.	SPAIN AND TIE LEAGUE.
The SECRETARY-GENERAL said that M. Quinones de Leon was very happy at the decision of his Government to take part in the next Assembly. The Secretary-General presumed that no difficulty regarding Spain’s election to the Council and her re-eligibility was to be anticipated. There ought not to be any, as the whole scheme of re-eligibility had been specially arranged for Spain.
4.	FIFTH SESSION OF THE PREPARATORY COMMISSION FOR THE I) ISARMAMSN T CQNFERBN QE .
The SECRETARY-GENERAL had noticed very severe press .comments on the alleged failure of the Preparatory Committee.
’'vhile it was true that very little effective progress had been made, he could not take the pessimistic view that the value of the League had been diminished. People expected too muoh progress in a question which depended on the state of publio opinion.
-3-
Some of the best supporters cf the League were inclined to take out one article of the Covenant and over-emphasiae it.
It might be said that preservation of peace was infinitely more important than disarmament, though of course there was a certain connection between the two. The present prase oampaign, in England particularly, was doing harm. Ee pointed out the analogy which could be found in the history of religions where all schisms arose from over-emphasis on e particular point to the total negleot of others.
M. AVEHOL agreed with the Secretary-General.
M. COLBM agreed with what the Secretary-General had. said , but he was afraid that if one emphasized the point that disarmament was only one aspect - although a very important one - of the activities of the League one would be accused of presenting a defenoe after an actual failure or in view of a possible one. It was reoess&ry to be very careful not to give way too far and to give the impression that disarmament is considered by us to be less important than it really is. Public opinion expected the League to do something in the matter of disarmament. It had been worked up overmuch by rather rash resolutions of the Assembly. This exposed the League to criticism at the present time when it became apparent that an immediate solution oould not be found. In these circumstances, it was necessary for the League to stiok firmly to Article 8 of the Covenant and to work for its practical execution and not to accept failure. After only a few weeks' study of tb9 question he felt rather optimistic, and what happened at the last session had not destroyed his belief that in spite of conditions prevailing in Europe something of substantial value might be achieved in a not very distant future. Too much importance was not to be attached to press oritioisms.
4
The SECRETARY-GENERAL agreed that disarmament was a very important part of the Leaguers work, hut he' was afraid that in some countries - in England especially - certain people took the view that nothing mattered but disarmament.
He agreed that the League would and could ultimately carry out Article 8.
Mr, SlEETSER agreed with M. Colban that disproportionate importance had been attached to the meeting by the press and that too great optimism had been raised by the Assembly.
Marquis PAULUCCI El CAEBOLI BASQUE said that in his view what had taken place was the failure of what might be called the demagogio stage in the work of disarmament. Some of the best friends of the League had been making poetry about disarmament, and this was the transition from poetry to prose.
Mr. SWEET SEE said that there was a great danger to the League in the fact that some members of the Preparatory Committee laid wholly undue emphasis on sanctions and the "responsibilities” of the League.
M. CO LEAN agreed that it was a great mistake to present the Covenant as a bar to disarmament, as had been done by certain speakers at the Preparatory Committee.
Mr. SWEETSER said that in his judgment the Covenant had also been wrongly used to oppose Mr. Kellogg’s proposals regarding the outlawry of war.
The SECRETARY-GENERAL thought that Mr. Kellogg’s proposal had been criticised on the ground that there was nothing to mate it binding.
5
Mr. SYELTSER agreed that this was the criticism put forward now, but previously the French Ilote was such as to elicit a reply from Mr. Kellogg to the effect that he hoped it was not true that the League was against outlawry of war.
The danger lay in the League being caught between the two extremes, on the one hand the Russian proposals for complete
i
disarmament and on the other the American proposal to outlaw war, both of which in some quarters were held inacceptable largely and, he thought, unwisely, beoause of the sanctions provided in the Covenant. Some members of the Preparatory Committee might quite well give a different interpretation to the Covenant in these matters, as, after all, its fundamental purpose was peace.
5.	THE LEAGUE LIBRARY.
Marquis PÂULUCCI LI CALBOLI BÆùLLb said that as a consequence of the acceptance of the Rockefeller gift there had been a consultation by a committee of experts on the question of the Library. The Committee had examined the plan submitted by the architects. The plan could not be very detailed, of course, as the only indication which the architects had received was that the Library might be built on a site measuring 2,000 square metres. The Committee of Experts found that the allowance of 2,000 square metres vías insufficient, and were of opinion that 3,000 square metres would be necessary. Moreover they objected to the proposed site, and pointed out its deficiencies - it was too close to the Rue de Lausanne, úoo noisy, and the books would be exposed to too much dust.
As no better site was available on the ground owned at present by the League the Committee of experts had unan Piously declared that it was necessary to procure bbher ground between the League ground and the International Labour Office building. Their report had been submitted to the Committee of Live, who had accepted its conclusions.	As more ground was
«« 6
accordingly necessary the Committee of Fire had advised that the required sum for purchase should he taken from the Rockefeller donation, after of course Mr. Rockefeller had been approached, v/hen Mr. Rockefeller*s reply was received a new clan would he prepared in order to give the Council, and finally the Assembly, the ne cess ary elements for a decision»
Mr« FSIiCIH said the Librarian had thought out a system for the division of the Library into several special libraries -one for law, another for economics, ahother for social work.
This was desirable particularly from the point of view of economics, as there would be a special librarian for it» He asked whether it was definitely contemplated that the library of the International Labour Office should be incorporated in the League Library. Ha thought the answer depended on the question whether there would or would not be a social section of the library. He thought it would be useful to do everything in the planning of the Library which would make it easier for the international Labour Organisation to incorporate its library in the League Library.
Marquis PAÜLUCCI di CAIRO LI BAROEË said that that point had. been kept in view . . It was contemplated that there would be a special room for th9 economic section, another for the social section, and another for political and diplomatic history, and that the International Labour Organisation wen Id give to the social section of the Library the greater part of its books »while keeping those which were necessary for current work
Mr. S^SSTSER said that the Secretary-General had sent a request to all the Directors asking them for their proposals in regard to the Library. Unfortunately the request had been sent at the time of the Council and only two or three answers had
been received
7
Mr« FELKIN said one of the important points was to fill the present gaps in the library. He thought that it would be useful, as soon as the new library was started, to consult specialists - lawyers, economists and so on - who would indicate the lines on which the Library should develop.
A plan of this hind would usefully supplement the bibliographical
methods used by librarians. The value of a library was to have particular books in a particular order. He gave as an example
the London Library.
The SECRETARY- GENERAL hoped that the Committee of Experts would be in a position to advise regarding the future development of the Library. They had not been in a position to do so in May because, as Mr. Sweetser had pointed out, the various memoranda had not yet come in from the Sections. It would be useful for Mr, Sweetser to send a reminder of this note.
RSPRBSSLTAT10IT OF THE UNITED ST;.TBS OH THE OPIUM COMMITTEE.

A second consideration was that permission for Brazil to remain, as of right, in the Technical Organisations might tend towards that loosening of the LeagueTs fabric, of which ha already saw dangers ^ The preservation ef the League *s unity seemed to demand that s State which went cut should be really out.

If Brazil want'3d to tahe a part -6,g., in the Transit Organisation - it could do so only by a decision of the Transit Conference. The question of membership of the International Lab ou r Organisation, though legally simpler - for just as membership of the League involved membership ox the Organisation, cessation of the first must involve cassation of the latter - was oolitic ally more delicate»
A further reason for deprecating any attempt xo make it easy to remain was that there was a growing tendency by some States to get what they could out of the League without thinking of their obligations to it.

Brazil had proposed that her International Centre of Leprosy should work under the auspices of the League and the Council and Assembly had approved of this policy, which had now been so defined by the Health Committee that the Committee would be able to make use of the Institute without incurring any obligations towards it. 2e DUPLICATE PRINTING ADD DISTPIBUTTON OF DOCUMENTS,
(Directors1 Meeting Papers 7, 7(a) and 7(10).
The meeting first considered the proposal to discontinue the issue of separate extracts from the Report and Supplementary Report on the work of the Council (7(h)).
Mr. EELKIN said that hitherto the Economic Committee had insisted on making a special report to the Assembly, other than what appeared in the Report on the Work of the Council. If the present proposal, which he favoured, were adopted the Committee might he induced to forego its special report.
It was pointed out that the proposal would not lead
to any big saving, as specialists, who at present required only the relevant extracts from the Report, would in future require the Report in full.
M. 3UGTMURA thought that separate extracts were more useful for distribution to the various Ministries.
The MARQUIS PAULUOCI DI CALBOLI BA ROUE said that he would have liked the change to be made, but he would fall in with the general opinion of his colleagues•-
It was agreed that the present system should oontinue.
Circulation of Reports of Advisory Committees to Members of the League at the same time as their Circulation to the Council. (7, 5).
The SECRETARY-UEUERaL drew attention to certain disadvantages of tho procedure proposed. It might be unobjectionable in the c0se of purely technical Committees, tut there were others, such as the Permanent Mandates Commission,
s
the Permanent Advisory Committee on Military, Naval and Air questions, and, to a lees extent, the Opium Committee, whose reports lore directly on individual Governments« It seemed improper that these reports should he circulated as a general rule before the Council had consented» The Council had, in
fact, always shown some reluctance to authorise the simultaneous circulation of reports adopted between June and September,
although there were special reasons in that case.
Dr» RAJCHMM fully agreed and added that it was Lard enough as it was to heop Advisory Committees aware of tie fact that they were advisory and not executive organs,,
Mr. JACKilN said that the report of the Publications Committee was occasioned by the Assemblyrs criticism of alleged extravagance by the League in its publications. He had examined the question carefully and had concluded that such extravagance as there might be resided not in the extent of the leaguers publications but in their duplication. The Committee wished to avoid the re-distribution of documents.
The SECRETARY-GENE MAI agreed in principle, but jointed out with reference to the proposal then before the meeting that the only duplication involved in the present S7stem was that the members of the Council received the same -sport twice„

3,	SuporvjsQ'ty Committee; The SECRETARY-GENERAL said that the recent meeting of the Supervisory Commission had been difficult as the budget showed an increase of two millions, due, so far as the Secretariat was concerned, almost entirely to the Opium Board and the Economic and Transit Organizations and the Codification Conference,
In its introductory note the Committee said that if the Assembly was prepared to adopt a budget of 27,000 ,000 francs it saw no objection to the present budget on strictly financial groundsa If, however, the Assembly decided to reduce the budget it should say exactly which parts of the proposed work should be dropped»
4.	Visit to Greece: M. SUGIMURA gave a short account of his recent mission in Greece. The establishment of internal tranquillity had drawn foreign capital into the country, there was much public work in progress and the refugees formed
a valuable addition to the population» As regards international problems most of the members of the Government seemed to be partisans of the league, and in any case none of the difficulties which still existed seemed to him insurmountable.

QUESTIONS DIVERSES RELATIVES AU NOUVEAU BATIMENT.
Le SECRETAIRE GENERAL dit qu'il a jugé utile de convoquer les directeurs en séance extraordinaire pour examiner certaines questions posées au Secrétariat par les architectes actuellement réunis à Genève.
Le Marquis PAULUCCI di CALBOLI BARONE dit que la question principale est celle de l'emplacement des différents batiments. A supposer que l'on dispose de toute la propriété Barton, deux solutions sont possible :
O
O
Dans la solution H’ 1 la bibliothèque formerait un batiment spécia.l qui serait placé entre le Secrétariat et le Bureau international du Travail, puis viendrait le bâtiment du Secrétariat, puis celui des Assemblées. Cette solution, qui est la plus logique, au point de vue de l1 emplacement de la bibliothèque, présente comme désavantage quTune partie du Secrétariat serait exposée à la bise et que l'ensemble serait assez laid, au point de vue architecturale
La solution H° 2 prévoit un plan différent : bibliothèque, puis salie des assemblées, puis secrétariat. Cette solution aurait l'avantage de donner au Secrétariat la meilleure exposition possible, mile fournit aussi la possibilité d'agrandir facilement le Secrétariat, mais il faudrait 120 mètres de couloir pour rejoindre la bibliothèque au Secrétariat,
le SECRETAIRE GERERAI consulte les membres du Comité pour savoir si ce désavantage du plan II0 2 doit nécessairement le faire écarter.
Après un échange de vues, les Membres du Comité sont d’accord pour recommander le plan N° 2.
Le Marquis PAULTJCCI di CALBOLI BARGUE dit que la seconde question est celle de la distribution des salles de
commissions.
D'après le programme, ces salles doivent etre réparties en deux groupes, un de chaque coté de la salle de l'Assemblée, les architectes se demandent s'il ne serait pas préférable de faire un seul groupe comprenant les salles de commissions et la salle du Conseil.
le SECRETAIRE GERERAI et les membres du Comité sont d'accord pour préférer que la salle du Conseil et trois ou quatre i salles de commissions soient d’un coté, (coté Secrétariat) et les autres salles de l'autre.
- 3 -
Le Marquis PAULüCuï di CALBOLI BAR O IIS dit qus la troisième question posée est celle de la grandeur de la salle des Assemblées.
Les architectes craignent qu7étant donné le grand
nombre de places demandées par le Secrétariat, la salle ne soit
comprend
presque vide. La salle de la Réformation/218 places avec pupitres pour les délégués, 180 places sans pupitres pour leurs secrétaires. Or, le secrétariat demande pour la nouvelle salle 400 places avec pupitres pour les délégués et 300 places pour les secrétaires. Il en résulterait que la salle aurait un diamètre de 47 mètres.
M„ LLOYD explique que la salle de la Réformation a 570 m2 et que la salle projetée en aurait 1800.
Le Marquis PA.ULUC0I di CaIBOLI EA.ROEE ajoute quTon a demandé que la nouvelle salle comprenne 1,000 places pour le public ( il y en a 468 à la Salle de la Réformation) et 600 places avec pupitres pour les journalistes (à la Salle de la Réformation : 212),
Le SECRETAIRE GEEERAL est dravis qu'il y a lieu de constituer un petit comité qui sera chargé de reviser les chiffres qui avaient été proposés. Feront partie de ce comité le Marquis Paulucci di Calboli Barone, un membre de la Section d'information et Mr, Wilson,
Le Marquis PAULUOCI ài CALBOLI BARCEE dit que les architectes désireraient aussi que le Secrétariat étudie la question de la disposition des pupitres des délégués dans la
salle des séances.
4
Le programme prévoit deux délégués par pupitre; les architectes font remarquer que si l'on maintenait les dispositions de la salle de la Réformation, c'est-à-dire quatre délégués par pupitre, on économiserait de la place. Dans l'opinion des architectes, les pupitres devraient être fixes.
M. AVENOL est d'avis de demander aux architectes de calculer la différence en qui résulterait de l'emploi de pupitres pour quatre délégués au lieu de pupitres pour deux délégués.
M. COM&Ky est d'avis de ne pas fixer les pupitres pour conserver à la salle l'élasticité nécessaire.
Le SECRETAIRE GENERAL dit que oe"hte question sera également renvoyée au petit comité qui vient d'être constitué.
Le Marquis PAULUCCI di CAiBOLI BARONE dit que la dernière question posée par les architectes est celle de la salle du Conseil. Ils croient que le Secrétariat, en demandant une salle de 20 mètres sur 10 mètres demande une salle trop petite, car ce sont là les dimensions de la salle vitrée actuelle. Il est vrai que la nouvelle salle du Conseil aurait également une galerie contenant 120 places, destinée au public, mais une salle de 20 mètres sur 10 mètres ne permettrait d'installer que 60 places avec pupitres ou 100 places sans pupitres pour les journalistes.
M. COMERT dit qu'il y a lieu de prévoir 100 places pour les journalistes. Il propose d'étudier la possibilité de leur donner chacun un fauteuil muni d'une tablette.
Le SECRETAIRE GENERAL indique qu'il y aura lieu de prévoir dans la salle 18 places pour les membres du Conseil, 30 places pour leurs secrétaires, 30 pour le secrétariat et 100 pour les journalistes, la tribune étant en principe réservée au public et aux agents diplomatiques. Mais le Comité qui vient d'être consti tué pourrait aussi étudier cette question.

The Acting Secretary-General, referring to the last session of the Councilj said that of the throe most interesting questions before it, one, the St. Gotthardt, had hoen sottled, but the other two, the Optants question and the Polish-Lithuaniam disputo, had not been settled.. He felt that he should draw attention to this, as at previous Directors1 Meetings it had been questioned whether it mattered if Foreign Ministers attended the Council sessions or not.

Y7ould disGues whether they should move their headquarters from Brussels to Geneva. Lame Rachel CRCWDY thought that the move would increase tho existing confusion in the mind of the general public between the League and the League of nations Union.
Mr. IORIfRAY said that, who re as at Brussels the Union could exereise a osrtain influence on public opinion, it could not do so to the some extent at Geneva, The League alroady influenced publio opinion to whatever extent it could be influenced from Geneva.
The Acting Secrotary-General thought that this was a good argument, and one which oould be urgod upon the Union, v/hereas it might be diffiault to state some of the others
Mr. WALTERS thought that the move should bo discouraged M. ROSTING thought that it had not mattered much that the Inter-Parliamentary Union had its headquarters at Geneva.

ary for the Council to deal with this question. Should it be taken off the agenda, or should the Rapporteur inform the Council that there was no need for it to be considered ?
The SECRETARY-GENERAL preferred the latter course.

Items 51, 52, and 55.

II. The resting considered the arrangements for the 1,'inth Ordinary Session of the Assembly (directors1 Leeting paper 17).
One or two changes were made, which II.de Iiontenach was ashed t® embody in. a revised edition of the Mote to be issued as a special circular.
The follov/ing points also arose in the discussion :
Mr.GOLEAL proposed that the Note should contain a statement t© the effect that Assembly Committees 1, 2 and 6 could meet simultaneously, and Committees 3, 4 and 5.
Item 11 of the AssemblyTs Agenda. (Alcoholism).
Lr.RAJCHMAN asked if it was inevitable thar the Assembly should begin the discussion of the question ab ovo. A first proposal had already been made to the effect that the Council should nominate an expert committee to consider such alcohol questions as were within the competence of the league.
The SECRETARY-GENERAL thought that the earlier discussions need net be repeated. The debate might begin by the Rapporteur recalling the previous proposal.
Mr.COLBidS thought it essential that someone should insist en a serious consideration of the question whether these matters were within the competence of the League.
131.RAJCKMAN and SALTER having suggested that the question should be treated as late as possible by the Second Committee, in order to prevent its encroaching on the other business of the Committee, the SECRETARY-GENERAL pointed out that in so far as it was a League question at all it concerned questions of
3~
¡Haggling and territorial waters, and that the First Committee would, robably therefore have to be consulted with regard to it. If so, he Second Committee should net leave it right till the end.
Sir Arthur SALTER thought that at future sessions of the nssem-ly it might be necessary to divide the work now given solely to the Second Committee. The Members of the other Committees were all supposed to be experts in a given thing, legal questions, disarmament, etc., but the Members of the Second Committee were experts only in being expert.
The SECRETARY-GENERAL thought that any revolutionary change could be avoided by transferring some of the work to the Sixth Committee.
For instance Item 11 might well be treated by the Sixth Committee.
Ee proposed, however, to leave the item under the Second Committee as it was at present, but hoped that some delegate might propose its transfer to the Sixth Committee. For Instance, the President of the Second Committee might ask the General Committee to hand it over to the Sixth Committee.
Item 12. Relations between the League of Eat ions and Institutes or Eddies set up under its authority.
It was agreed that this should go to the Second Committee.
Item 9. Construction of an Assembly Hall.
It was agz*eed that the Council would probably do no more than to transmit the report of the Committee cf Five to the Assembly.
The item should first be sent to the General Committee in order to decide upon the procedure only. It would be best if the General Committee then sent it to the Fourth Committee.
Sir Arthur SALTER hoped that the Fourth Committee would not be faoed with alternative proposals. It was not competent to choose between two alternative policies, but only to say if the money should

Minutes of the Directors Meeting held on Wednesday, August 29th, 1928 at 3.50 g.m.
Present:	Secretary-General
The Deputy Secretary-General MM. Marquis Paulucci di Calbcli Dufour Feronce Sugimura

1. The meeting considered the question of the representation of the Secretariat at the Barcelona Exhibition (D.M.Paper 19 and
L.A.M. Papers 17 and 17a) .
The SECRETARY GERERAL said that the reason advanced in favour of representation was that of bringing the League to the notice of the many Latin-Americans who would visit the exhibition.
On the other hand, he feared that a most awkward precedent might be created. The representation of the Secretariat at the Technical Press Exhibition at Cologne could not be quoted as a precedent Decause of the special nature of the exhibition, and he had insisted upon this then he had agreed to it.
M.	COMERT agreed that the situation was not prejudiced M the Cologne Exhibition. The Information Section had useful material to display at exhibitions and he had no doubt that to süov- this at Barcelona would be a valuable means of propaganda.
-2-
ge proposed that the question should be put to the Fourth Committee of the Assembly.
K. Dl'FOUR FEEOIICE spoke as one who had had experience in exhibiting. His experience was that the exhibitor either got a aecLal or did not get a medal, but that he got nothing more. If this was all that an industrialist got from exhibiting at general exhibitions of the Barcelona kind, he very much doubted whether the league would gain more.
The DEPUTY SECAETAEY-UEIJLIuJj agreed t nax i*gpr*6 S6n Xc±x ion at Barcelona was undesirable. If it was accepted the precedent was bound to be invoked.
The Marquis FAULUCCI DI CALBOLI admitted that representation might benefit the League's propaganda with Latin Americans but this propaganda could be done by other means and it seemed that the price of accepting representation was too great. Moreover, it tas not in keeping with the League's functions as an official organisation. Lastly, is was an inopportune moment to ask the Fourth Committee for money.
Sir Arthur SALTER was opposed to representation at general exhibitions. The League would appear to be demonstrating to the casual observer what a good institution it was; this seemed to be the function of the League of nations Unions, On the other hand, representation at some special exnioitions where there might be a real interest in knowing what the League was

doing in a particular field should be more sympathetically considered.
Monsieur BUZRO said that the Latin American
Governments would be represented at the exhibition by their
commercial agents and he did not wish to deny that the
exhibition might be useful for propaganda in Latin America.
On the other hand this use should not be exaggerated and he
a
did not think that it would igustify/representation undesirable on ot..er grounds. Moreover, the essence of successful propaganda in Latin America was that it should be done according to the individual needs of each country. It might indeed be difficult to make a negative reply to the invitation of the Mayor cf Barcelona, but some satisfaction might be given if some member of the Secretariat could be sent teat tend the exhibition.
M. COIfERT said that he did not understand the distinction between general and technical exhibitions made by Sir Arthur Salter. ras not the League & "selling its at technical exhibitions also?
wares
-4-
Sir ¿RTHU3 S' LT:-R maintained the distinction. The object of représentât ion at a general exhibition would be to advertise the value of the League as such, while this could not be the case at a technical exhibition. To take a national analogy the British Ministry of Health might be represented at a technical health exhibition, but at a general exhibition the British Government woul not undertake to demonstrate the excellence of the British constitution.
Dr. RA.JCHI/KN said that the Health Organisation had narti-cipated in four exhibitions which hau given different results, it one, the Health Exhibition at Dusselurf, representation had been useful because the exhibition had been really technical.
At another, at Seville, it hau been much less useful because the exhibition had been commercial and touristic, rather than technical;. He concluded that representation at some technical exhibitions would be useful, at others, not, am. agreed with Sir Arthur Suiter that it was for the League of Rations Unions to promote League propaganaa at general exhibitions. He thought that the Information Section might have skeleton exhibits which the League of Nations Unions could use if they wished to.
Monsieur HALS felt that though"ho League should not be represented, it should do what it could to give some satisfaction to the City of Barcelona which hau shown sreat courtesy to the League the time of the Transit Conference.
The Deputy SECT-. ETA LY G/.IF HAL thought that these could be recalled in the letter of reply.
'urteeies
\
Mr* JAOKLIU contended that the Secretariat had been represented at the Gologne Press Exhibition not so much for the purpose of propaganda as in the exercise of the ordinary functions of administration and that this should be the test in accepting invitations to exhibitions»
The SB ORE If RY -GPU: ;RA 1 sunned up as follows:
(1)	The Leagu.e should not be represented at general exhibitions:
(2)	Representation at technical exhibit:' ns should be considered according to the merits of the particular case*.
(2) M. Comert shoulu draft for his signature the politest possible reply to Barcelona, giving the reasons for refusal, recalling the debt which the League owed to the city and offering to send lecturers if they were wanted.
In reply to M» Gomert, the Secretary-Gen ral said that he wculu not be prepared to go himself to Barcelona as this, too, would create a diificult precedent. He agreed, however, favourably to consider sending some members of the Secretariat to the Exhibition for lecturing purposes, if lectures were included in the Exhibition programme.

1,	The SECEETARY-GEHEBAL said that there were one cr two
points arising out of the last session of the Assembly which it might be useful to discuss. The first was the difficulty of getting speakers to-open the general debate on the Tuesday afternoon, It had been suggested to him that the general
>
debate might be suppressed as the discussion of the reports of the Committees would give an opportunity to say what would have been said in the general debate. He did not like this suggestion. Alternatively, he would propose that the Committees might sit on the Tuesday afternoon and following afternoons and the debate begin on the Wednesday morning, when it should certainly be possible to find speakers to open the debate and continue on the other mornings only. Another reason in favour of this procedure was that it was desirable to shorten the sessions of the Assembly and the only way to do this was to get the Committees to work earlier.
-2-
M» COMERS agreed that it would be fatal to suppress the general debate. What was necessary was that sene important delegate should be induced to open the debate. If that happened other speakers would readily follow.
MoSUGJlaUHA said that some delegates arrived at Geneva with their speeches in their pockets and that if the Secretariat worked hard it should be able to find speakers for the Tuesday afternoon. It might be wise to avoid making a break in the tradition.
The SECRETARY-GENERAL said that he still felt that it might be an improvement if the Committees get to work earlier.
The SECRETílRY-GSITERhl said that the instructions which he had given regarding order on the Assembly platform had not had their full effect. Members of the Secretariat should remember that they v/ere very conspicuous on the platform and that in consequence any movement on the platform was particularly disturbing to delegates. In future it would be necessary to reserve mere seats for the Secretariat in the space behind the delegates1 seats. In order to make room both for the Secretariat and the experts it might be necessary tc exclude the public from the ground floor.
The SECRETARY-GENERAL, referring to the trouble to which the increase in the Budget had given rise, said that he did not fear any serious cor.B9q.ue no es if next yearTs budget showed no increase, so that the claim he had made that the present yearTs increase was exceptional would be accepted. He asked for the assistance of all Directors in this respect.
-3-
SUGiLIüRà said that if the woik of the League was to oe developed, especially in the extra-European countries, money was necessary* If such work was to be done there must therefore either be an increase of the Budget or a redistribution c.f the credits within it. He remarked a tendency which seemed to him unfair to expect the -Suâtes specially interested to contribute towards the cost of any work which was undertaken by the League outside Europe.
The S ECLELAuY-LELEFuLL presumed that ILSugimura's remarks applied specially to the proposal for the Opium Enquiry in the Far East* He thought, however, that the difficult inexperienced in finding the credits for this enquiry was due to the late hour at which it had been proposed* He felt sure that if due notice were given cf proposals for special work or special missions particularly in distant regions the money would be forthcoming. Yfnat the Assembly dreaded was a continuing rapid increase in the cost of the regular work of the League.

1 * NOUVELLE METHODS D1INFORMATION. POUR LA PRESSE (Directors’ Meeting Paper 21. 1928).
M* COMERT dit que le succès de l’expérience tentée avec la collaboration de LL Rueff avant la réunion de la Conférence de la Double imposition lui a donné l’idée de proposer d'étendre à d'autres conférences le système des exposés à la presse, par des représentants des sections techniques.
Un exposé fait une dizaine de jours à l’avance, permet aux journalistes de se rendre compte exactement de l'intérêt du travail qui va être fait à une conférence.
8
Mr, CATASTINI signale qu'il y aurait des difficultés pour appliquer cette procédure aux réunions de la Commission des mandats.
On ne peut évidemment anticiper ni les observations qui seraient even— tuellement présentées sur les rapports des Puissances mandataires ni les conclusions de la Commission, Les questions traitées étant souvent très délicates, le silence est la meilleure politique.
LE SECRETAIRE GENERAL fait remarquer que la proposition de L, Comert ne vise nullement les conclusions éventuelles d'une Commission« Il est possible, d'ailleurs, que pour des Commissions comme la Commission des Mandats, on ne puisse donner,, ayant la session, autre chose qu'un communiqué*
Mo DUFOUR-FERONCE appuie la proposition de L. Comert. Le système proposé est appliqué depuis de nombreuses années au Ministère des Affaires Etrangères à Berlin, où, avant une réunion technique, le Service de presse demande à un expert nfappartenant pas à ce service, de faire un exposé aux journalistes. Cet expert donne à la presse les explications qu'il croit utile de lui donner.
M. SUGIMURA fait remarquer que dans le cas mentionné par M. Dufour-Feronce, le fonctionnaire parlant à des journalistes peut expose une thèse nationale. Au Secrétariat, il est nécessaire d’être tout à 'ait objectif, M, Comert, dans son mémorandum, a parlé "d’informations générales, mais d'un caractère un peu confidentiel**. Il pense que M, Comert ne visait qu’une information parfaitement impartiale.
D’ailleurs, la première tâche du Secrétariat est d'assurer le succès des conférences, L’information des journalistes ne peut venir qu'en second lieu. Il proposerait donc que le nouveau système d’information à la presse ne reçoive pas uns sanction du Secrétaire général qui pourrait inquiéter les délégations, mais qu’il soit appliqué, le üô.s échéant.
Ccmert ne
M» AVENOL fait remarquer que la proposition de M. vise qu’à faire expliquer à la presse des questions techniques. Il ne s'agit pas de faire un exposé à la presse avant le Conseil ou avant la réunion de la Commission du désarmement, par exemple, mais, au cours de l'année, la Société des dations réunit de nombreuses conférences techniques qui ne sont d'ailleurs souvent que des conférences d'experts et non pas des conférences de délégués de gouvernements» Les journalistes ne sont pas préparés à étudier cos questions très techniques. Quelques explications orales peuvent les aider à comprendre la portée de la conférence et les points qui y seront di scutês»
M* SUGIMURA fait remarquer que, lorsque des délégations sont profondément divisées sur des questions techniques, celles-ci cessent d'être purement techniques. La Conférence de la double imposition elle-même a fourni un exemple de ces divergences qui dépassent le domaine technique. D'oû la nécessité de nô faire qu'un exposé parfaitement objectif.
LE jüAEQUIS PAULUCCI DI CAL30LI BARONS trouve la proposition de M, Comert très bonne» Il est cependant d’avis de no pas l'adopter
comme un système général, mais de l'employer le cas échéant, quand
de -
«lie ne risque pas de faire plus/mal que de bien. Le système peut être utile si la personne chargée de faire l'exposé connaît très bien le milieu et reste tout à fait objective.
M» COLBAII trouve la projet excellent. Les conférences travaillent sur des documents volumineux que les journalistes reçoivent très tard. Il est donc utile de leur donner une sorte d’introduction à la documentation de la conférence. l'a Colban est aussi d'accord que les journalistes ne doivent pas dire qu'on leur a fait un exposé et le publier comme une déclaration du Secrétariat.
Pour des questions très délicates, on pourrait s'en tenir à la procédure qui consiste à faire donner des explications
4
par la -Section compétente aux membres de la Section d ' Information qui, ensuite, peuvent individuellement aider les journalistes,
Mo FELXIîi pon-ie que la proposition de Me Cornert est très utile pour des sections techniques comme la section économique, mais doute qu’on puisse l’appliquer systématiquement0 11 serait difficile de. le faire pour dos réunions da Comité financier et du Comité économique. a.-ême une réunion qui paraît purement technique comme celle des experts vétérinaires, soulève de vives passions politiques. Il ne faut donc pas que les journalistes croient qu’ils auront, avant chaque réunion technique, un exposé fait par un membre de la section compétente.
LE SECRETAIRE GENERAL pense que l’on pourrait adopter la procédure suivante: la Section d’information sachant qu’une réunion va avoir lieu, s'adressera, avant cette réunion, au Directeur de la Section compétence pour lui demander s'il juge utile de donner un expose à la presse. C’9st le Directeur de la Section compétente qui décidera et il est certain qu’il ne prendra pas de décision qui puisse nuire au succès de la conférence.
Mo SUGILüRa insiste sur le fait que les délégations -surtout cellesqui viennent de loin- craignent souvent de trouver à Genève une atmosphère déjà créée. Il est nécessaire de ne rien faire qui puisse confirmer ces soupçons et il paraît, en général, préférable que des informations soient transmises à la presse par la Section d’informât!a
Mo HIAM est d’accord ar'ec M. Felkln. Il ne pense pas qu’il soit utile que les correspondants de presse aient l’impression qu’ils entendront dans chaque cas un exposé du représentant de la Section compétente ot qu’ils aient, par suite, des soupçons si cet exposé n’a pas lieu.
5
M. COIïERT approuve les diverses objections qui ont été présentées et est entièrement d’accord avec le Secrétaire général qui a proposé que ce soit le directeur de la Section technique compétente qui décide, Il fait remarquer que la procédure qu’il a proposée présente beaucoup moins de dangers que les conversations de journalistes avec des membres de Section autres que ceux de la Section d'information.
M* CATASTIïïI pense qu’il y aurait intérêt à maintenir le principe que les ilembres de Sections autres que la Section d’information ne doivent pas avoir, en général, ^e conversations avec les journalistes sur les affaires de la Société des Hâtions. C'est pourquoi, même pour la publicité des questions techniques, il préférerait en règle générale, que le directeur de la Section compétente communique ses idées à la presse par la voie de la Section ¿'Information.
M* COMERT dit que l’expérience prouve qu’il y a, en effet, les plus grands dangers à laisser des fonotionnaores qui ne sont pas professionnellement, comme ceux de la Section d'information, en contact quotidien avec la presse, communiquer avec les journalistes. Il n’y a eu que trop d’exemples d’indiscrétions commises de cette manière. Un fonctionnaire peut, par exemple, être tenté de se faire ainsi une publicité personnelle.
Cependant, en cq qui concerne Iss exposés de questions techniques, il fait remarquer que les journalistes n’accepteraient pas ct'écouter un membre de la Section d ’ information qui leur ferait une conférence sur l’unification des statistiques par exemple, tandis qu'ils écouteront volontiers le représentent de la section compétente lorsqu’il essaiera de leur faire comprendre 1'intérêt de ce problème technique.

Note by the Secretary-General,
The following report, with the	¿^rail's
note thereon, is circulated to Under Secretaries-General, directors ano heads of Sections.
It will be on the agenda of an approaching Directors' meeting.
Report of the Committee appointed to consider questions concerning Communications between the President of the Council, the Secretary-General and the Parties to a dispute in case of emergency.
The Committee appointed by the Secretary-General, consisting of Marquis Paulucci (President), A. Haas, U, Roddolo, M. Vallery Radot, Mr. Walters, Mr. Huston and Mr. Buxton (Secretary), considered the points raised in hr. Bur ton's memorandum and the notes thereon by the Secretary-General and Mr. Walters. The Committee's report is unanimous.
The Committee considers that the Secretariat should take precautions at least equal to those taken by a loreign Office cr a War Office, to ensure that important communications are- opened and reach the proper authorities with the least possible delay at whatever hour they arrive. It therefore proposes that a list of members of Section should be prepared, who would cone on duty in turn for one week at a time ( from Saturday to Saturday)* from 7.SO p.m. until 9 a.m. the following day and all day on Sundays and holidays. It would be the duty of these officials to open telegrams and letters sent by hand addressed to the Secretary-General and, in case of an urgent communication, to get into touch at once with the 3ecretary —General.	If the Secretary—General is not available
they should aet into touch with the Deputy Secretary-General, or failing him with the Senior Under Secretary-General available
2
A room should he furnished at the Secretariat in which the official could sleep and he TTould be responsible for having in that room the telephone address of: (l) the Secretary-General, the deputy Secretary-General and under Sscretaries-General and their secretaries; (2) the telephone and telegraph address of the Acting President of the Council (business address and private house) and of his deputy if one was appointed; ( à) a cony of the telegrams sent by the President ox the Council and the Secretary-General in previous eases of emergency.
Proposed regulations for these officials are attached to the present report.
The Committee suggests that these officials should receive- remuneration at the rate of 20 francs for night and 20 francs for day duty on a ¿Sunday or a holiday, and that in the case of day duty they should be given equivalent time off.	They would be notifieefof their
turn of duty by the Under Secretary-General in charge of Internal Administration. It would be the duty of the official coming on duty on Saturday to obtain information as to whether the Secretary-General himself would be available In C-eneva and if not who was acting as his substitute.
Mr. Huston is arranging a system by which our telephonists on leaving the Secretariat at 6 p.n. will connect an electric bell running from the tube by which the telegrams arrive to the concierge's desk. Aach cartridge carrying a telegram down the tube will on arriving at the post office automatically start a bell ringing until the night watchman has tame out the telegram and pushed an electric button notifying the central telegraph office that the telegram has arrived and that the line is free. In order to make sure that the cartridges do not remain stuck in the tube on their way to the Secretariat one of the lamps of the tube will remain lighted until the signal that the telegram has duly arrived at the office is given to the central telegraph office.
The cost of this installation will not be great.
As an -xtra precaution, however, the concierge should be instructed to look every half hour to see whether any telegram has arrived.	The concierge will immediately
hand to the official in charge any telegram ox letter sent by hand that arrives addressed to the Secretary-General or the League.
With regard to the other points raised in Mr. Buxton's memorandum, the Committee suggests: ( l) that the Secretary-General, the deputy Secretary-General or one of the Under 3e ere tax ie s-Ge ne xal should always be available to receive and deal with an urgent message; (2) that the Secretary-General should suggest in his report to the March Council that the President of the Council should ar ran »a with the Secretary-General at the beginning of his term of office that either he himself or a substitute appointed by him will always be on the telephone with C-eneva. The Secretariat should be in possession of the telephone and telegraph address of the office ant private house of
- 3 ~
the President and his substitute; (3) that the Secretary-general should refer in his report to paragraph III (b) in the report of the Committee of the Council, which reads:
"Even before the Council meets, it is desirable that the Acting President should send telegraphic appeals to the Parties to the dispute to refrain forthwith from any hostile acts.
The nature of this appeal will necessarily vary with the circumstances of each case.
If, owing to exceptional circumstances, the Secretary-General considered that the Acting President was not in a position to act, he might request the ex-President most recently in office who is available to take this step in the name of the Council.”
With regard to wireless, the Agence télégraphique suisse now telephones important news received by wireless or other channels to the Information Section. The Committee suggests that the Agence télégraphique suisse should be asked to telephone such news arriving between 7.30 p.m. and 9 a.m. and during the day on Sundays and holidays to the official in charge at the Secretariat.
With regard to a sign indicating emergency on communications from Members of the League, If. Haas pointed out that the Transit Committee ha£ already proposed that such communications be narked "Priorité nations" and that this recommendation, with others, had already been sent to Governments for their observations. As no objection had been received, this recommendation would shortly be put into force.
This report deals mainly with the avoidance of delay after an appeal has reached Geneva, but there are two 'points of extreme importance which should not be lost sight of;
(1)	the factor most essential for the rapidity of League action is the immediate despatch of the appeal by the Government concerned - e.g. the best reply to an ultimatum is an immediate appeal to the League;
(2)	the rapid transmission by telegraph of the message. On this point the Transit Committee has made a proposal which is being submitted to the Governments.
(Signed) PAULUCCI 1)1 CALBOLI BABOLE.
Geneva, January loth, 1928.

Regulations for the official in charge at the Secretariat fox the purpose of opening communications to the Secret ary-General ana getting into touch with him in cases of emergency.
(1)	You are on duty at the Secretariat from Saturday to Saturday, from 7.SO p.m. until 9 a.m. and all day on Sunday and during a recognised holiday. You can leave the Secretariat for meals on a Sunday or a holiday, provided you go to a place on the telephone with the Secretariat and within 10 minutes walk therefrom, and leave your telephone address with the concierge.
(2)	It is your duty always to inform the concierge where you will he in the building if not in the room allotted to you.
(3)	You are to open all telegrams and letters sent

ffOTE BY TRu SECRLTARY-UnRhRAL TO MARQUIS PRUIUCOI.
14th January, 1928.
The report of the Committee over which you presided concerning questions relating to Communications between the President of the Council, the Secretary-General and the Parties to a dispute in caso of emergency, appears to me excellent and acceptable in its entirety7.
As, however, all the Sections are interested in the matter, I would propose that the Report should be circulated to Under Secretaries-General, Directors and Chefs de Section* and that it should be placed cn the agenda of an approaching Directors1 Meeting for discussion.
I gather that the expense involved would be in the neighbourhood of 8,000 francs a year. We should,
I thirds:, submit the position to the Supervisory Commission when it meets, explaining that as the Council and various technical committees are endeavouring to find methods of saving minutes in case of an emergency, we feel bound to put the scheme which the Committee has evolved into force immediately, and we trust that the;, will see no objection from the financial point of view. I presume that there will be no difficulty in finding some budgetary arrangement' to furnish the necessary sura?

EXAMPLE 01 TliL AilOUhT OP DOCULMhTS CIRCULATED IOR Till
CONSIDERATION OP A C0.I2TTTLE,
It seems important that the Publications Committee should realise the amount of literature which members of League committees are at present expected to swallow.
The expense involved in producing that literature though no doubt an important matter seems to me secondary to the question of presenting to members of committees the material for their discussions in a form that- they can take in and
take in easily
2
To show what happens new I have collected and attach a copy of each oi the aoouraents so far circulated for a single meeting of a single sub-committee» The meeting is not till .¿arch lyth, but there are already about 350 pages of roneo and 170 pages of print and documents are still coming in. I think
gr
it would be worth while for someone who has had experience of committee work to read them and give his opinion as to whether it would be easy for a member of that committee to digest their contents and form his conclusions thereon.
In case the answer should be no, the remedy should I think be applied early in the preceedings and on the foil o v; i ng 1 i lie s.
1.	The new form of Council agenda with very short notes on each item should be adopted for committees in order to let the members see exactly what they are going to discuss.
2.	The secretary of the committee should endeavour to persuade the President to limit strictly at one session the amount and character of the material to be submitted on any subject to its next session;
(a)	by framing its requests for information in a way which will preclude long replies;
(b} by instructing either a rapporteur ox the Secretariat to condense the information collected on any question into a short, clear summary, the original imormation being available lor consultation but not circulated; (c) by emphasising the importance of conciseness in any report requested from outside sources.
3.	ilc committee resolution involving expenditure should be finally adopted until an estimate of its cost has been submitted to the Treasurer.
I admit that this may involve a good deal of trouble to the secretary of the committee to start with, but is not the alternative a drastic cut in the suns which 3eotions are at present allotted for publications?
( S igne d J A. BUAT01I,
J£
h. Sevensma is doing this.
IÆASU3 OF MATIONS.
Directors' Meeting Papar 4.1928»
GIRCULAII OH Cg IX GUMJIITS IF ONE LANGUAGE IN ADVANCE OF THE 0 ¡HER.
The following notice is circulated on the instructions ox the Secretary-General for discussion at the Directors' Meeting.
Bequests are frequently made for the circulation of one tart (French or English) of a document before the text in the other language is ready. It fairly often happens that in drafting the translation a mist ale a is discovered in the original which than may have to be xoneod again. Apart from this inconvenience it has always seemed to me as a matter of principle a mistake to circulate either the English text in advance of the French or the French text in advene« of the English, though there may be a few occasions of great urgency when it is necessary. If it is agreed that this should be very exceptional instructions might perhaps be given to the Sections to that effect.
(Signed) A, BUXTON.

Geneva, 16th March, 1928..
To the Secretary-General
The new form of Council agenda with short notes under each item explaining the question, devised by Sir Arthur Salter and Mr. Mcifinnon Wood and used for the first time at the last Council, seems to have pleased everybody both inside and outside the Secretariate It has saved the circulation of a number of rather annoying little documents, has I think let everybody see at a glance what is going to be discussed and has apparently given rise to no adverse criticism.
Would it not be a good plan to introduce it generally for all League committees and sub-committees?
(S ignedj A. Buxton.
16.3.28.

PUBLICATIONS COM. ilTTED.
PRINTING and DISTRIBUTION OF DOCUMENTS.
February 29th 1928.
7.
Budget:-
1.	The Budget is roneodd for the Secretary-General.
12.	Is printed in 50 copies for the Supervisory Oommi ssion.
3.	Is reprinted for the Council in June and distributed to
States Members as an Assembly document.
4.	Is reprinted in October as amended by the Assembly and
I sent to all States Members.
5.	Is reprinted as an annex to the Minutes of the Fourth Committee and sent to all States Members-
6.	Is reprinted in the Official Journal in January of the following year.
It was suggested that the budget should be issued as a separate number of the Official Journal, which would be published in October instead of January. This would avoid one distribution (5).
Reports of Committees to the Assembly.
Example Report of Fourth Committee.
1.	The Report is roneoed and submitted to the Fourth Committee
2.	Is printed and	distributed to	the Assembly
3.	The	Resolution	is printed	and	distributed separately
4.	The	Resolution	appears in	the	volume of Assembly Resolutions
5.	The	Resolution	appears in	the	verbatim records as distributed
in separate sheets
».	It appears in the Minutes of the Fourth Committee
7.	It appears in the Assembly Records (official Journal)
The Committee proposes that the separate printing of Assembly Resolutions as in 3 above be dropped as they are included in the reports and are also issued shortly after the Assembly collected in a volume.
-2-
Qffioial Journal (Council Minutes) .
Dr. Dixon gave the following example of the time it taxes at present to print the Official Journal. The December Oouncil Minutes were sent complete with annexes to the printer on December 23rd but the Journal was not issued till February 20th.
The Committee thinks this delay much too long and suggests that instructions should be given for the Journal always to have precedence over other documents.
Official Journal ( without Minutes) .
Dr. Dixon also explained that the following documents are printed separately and also appear in the ordinary issue of the Journal (not Council Minutes) ;
1.	A report on Counterfeiting Currency was printed separately in special type and format and had to be set up afresh for the Journal.
2.	Saar Governing Commission Quarterly Heports.
3.	Letters from Governments regarding Mandates received after the discussion of the Mandates report by the Council.
4.	Documents re gifts for the new building.
5.	Heports regarding Slavery.
The Committee proposes that all the above documents except the first should in future be circulated in the Journal alone.
Circulation of Advisory Committee Heports.
The Committee considers that the reports of Advisory Committees might well be circulated to States Members at the same time as they are distributed to the Council.
This would avoid a special circulation to Council as the reports would be circulated to Council and States Members at the s .e time.
The Heports would in that case already be in possession of the States Members when they receive the Minutes of Council at which the Heports are discussed.
The best way of getting this reform adopted would probably be by a recommendation from the Assembly to the Council.
The Committee will continue to consider whether further reprints
could not be avoided.

Note By the Secreta ry-Gensr al to the Puhl ications Committee.
As far as I can see, the recommendations contained in the Publications Committee Report (Directors' Meeting Paper 7* attached) on printing and distribution of documents, dated the 28th February 1926, are excellent.
The only observations which I have to offer are on 3. Is it really possible that the Official Journal should always have precedence over all other documents? I agree that the ganaral rula is right, but I presume that allowance can he made for possible exceptions.
Paragraph 4» No*4. It would seem to me to be wise to give separate circulation of offers of gifts from Government#* Mere printing in the Official Journal seems to me to be a,little lacking in cordiality, and it also has no effect whatever in stimulating other offers. Offers from private persons or &rg an is at ions ought not, I think, to be either ’circulated sr . printed in the Journal *	- .
Paragraph 5* It may be difficult for same of the Secretariat to the various Committees to accept this suggestion. But I hope that they will find it possible to do so, as I feel sure that it is s-eund. Would it not, however, be better that the Council itself should, if possible, take such a decision on its own initiative? A recommend at ion frcm the Assembly to the
Council would imply that the Members of the league were not altogether satisfied wi H the way in which the Council has up to now acted in the matter.
■ the President of the Obi
icaticn Committee.
