70TE 3Y TEE SECRETARY-GENERAL TO THE PU 311 CA T1017 S COMME ETES.
MARQUIS PAU IEC CI 0
Tour minute of the 24th April«
I naturally agree with the Publications Committeer3 suggestion that the word "always" in paragraph 3 of their report should be deleted.
A3 to my proposal that the Council should take a decision on its own initiative on the point raised in paragraph 5, perhaps, if the next Directors1 meeting is in general agreement, a short memorandum could be prepared, which I could circulate to the Members of the Council, and the matter might be placed on the Agenda as an administrative question. It would, perhaps, be voll to preface the memorandum by some short statement to the effect that the Publications (Committee of the Secretariat has been making recommendations to me for the purpose of instituting further economies as suggested by the Assembly.
I am, of course, ready to circulate the proposal with regard tc extracts of the annual reports on the Work of the Counoil to the various Directors for consideration« But I am a little doubtful as to whether it will meet with approval, since a concession has already been made by the various Committees in agreeing that the chapter concerning their annual reports should be considered as the special report presented by their Organisations tc the Assembly for consideration.
The original paper of the Publications Committee of the 29th February, 1928, my note thereon and your reply and this minute, might now be circulated for purposes of discussion at the next Directors' Meeting«

The Committee proposes that the separate printing of Assembly Resolutions as in 5 above be dropped as they are included in the reports and are also issued shortly after the Assembly
collected in a volume.
p:
Official Journal (Co-quoil Minutes).
Dr. Dixon gave the following example- of the tide it takes at present to print the Official Journal. The December Council Minutes were sent complete with annexes to the printer on December 23rd but the Journal was not issued till February 20th,
The Committee thinks this delay much too long and sugggsts that instructions should be given for the Journal always to have precedence over other documents.
Official Journal (without Minutes).
Dr. Dixon also explained that the following documents are printed separately and also appear in the ordinary issue of the Journal (not Council Minutes);
1.	A report on Counterfeiting Currency was printed separately in special type and format and had to be set up afresh for the Journal.
2.	Saar Governing Commission Quarterly Deports.
3.	letters from Governments regarding Mandates received after the discussion of the Mandates report by the Council.
4.	Documents re gifts for the new building*
5.	Reports regarding Slavery,
The Committee proposes that al 1 the above documents except the first should In lutaj6 be circulated in the .Journal alone.
Circulâtion of Advisory Committee Reports.
The Committee considers thac the reports oi Revisory Committees might well be circulated to States Ilembers at~~the same time as they are distributed to the Council.
This wauld avoid a special circulation to Council as the
reports would be circulated to Council and States Members at the
same time. The Reports would in that case already be in
possession of the States Members when they receive the Minutes of
Cauncil at which the Reports axe discussed.
The best way of getting this reform adopted would probably be
by a recommendation from the Assembly to th^ Council,
The Committee will continue to consider whether further
reprints could not be avoided.

TO THE S S OUST ARY - G-KN BRAD.
Proposal to suppress separate extracts of Report and Supplement ary Report on the Work of tho Counoil.
As I am mainly responsible for the suggestion that tho chapters in the Report and Supplementary Report on tho Work of the Counoil should not ho circulated separately, it may ho convenient if I st&to my reasons for making it. (See Directors' Meeting Papar 7 (a).
I think there is a good deal of advantage in concentrating Loaguo information into as few documents as possible and that in this particular casa it would ho simpler for an Assembly delegate, especially a delegate who attended more than one Committee, if his Bible consisted of nothing but tho Old and tho new Testament (tho Report and Supplementary Report), and if ho did not have to bother with any separate extracts from thorn.
I boliovo that that argument also holds good for a member of tho Press or of the general public, especially as there is no difficulty in finding the way about thoso two Reports.
It is not claimed that the saving in money would bo very large but as allowance has been made for perhaps more additional copies of tho Report- and Supplementary Report than would really bo requirod, tho estimate of a saving of 1000 francs is, I think, undor rather than over the mark. There is a considerable sale for the Reports, but not for the extracts.
My main argument, however, is simplification rather than economy.

Hote by the bee ret ary "general.
I circulate lor discussion at a future Directors Meeting an interesting minute from Dame Bach el Greedy, which contains the suggestion that it might he mall to draw up some general rules of procedure for league Committee as a whole.
Clearly, there has teen some abuse of procedure in the particular case of the Committee on the -Traffic in rfernen and Children and in that on Child welfare. It is clear that the Council intended each country te have one delegate, though of course we cannot object to that delegate bringing with him substitute delegates and technical advisers; but the two latter categories ought only to be al lowed to speak at the formal request of the head of the delegation. This rule has I believe always been taken for granted in other committees, and I think generally enforced.
If the discussion at the Directors Meeting is favourable to further study of the matter, I would propose to appoint a Committee of members of the Secretariat who have had experience of committee work to make definite proposals, though I rather doubt vhether it will be possible to lay down anything except very general rules. i'he Assembly procedure may, however, afford a useful guidance on certain points.
Whatever decision may be taken as to the general question, I would ask the Publications Committee to prepare a document for the use of Committee becret arias. Such a document wculd have the advantage not merely of making matters clear to
2
the G ce m it tees, but also of preventing mistaken statements as to restrictions being made to the members of committees.
The document should, I think, be drawn un not as a series of rules but in the form of a brief explanation .of the best way of procedure from the point of view of economy, etc., in each case. I am not surane of any rule by the Publications Committee that no document of mere than fifty pages should not be xoneoed, tut I believe that many years ago it was laid down such documents should, if possible, be printed, this being, the approximate point at which printing becomes more economical than r®neoing .
I should certainly be in favour of recommending, e.g., that meetings should not be held immediately after faster; since this is a date which is unfair to the Secretariat and ought not to be expected, I doubt, however, the possibility of arranging a hard and fast rule .

I do not -propose to put in a report on the work done by the traffic in '/Oman and Children Committee and the Child welfare Committee at their last meetings. Certain decisions, however, were taken which I think are of general interest to the Secretariat and one question arose which is also of some interest and on which I should like your o - inion and ad vie 9.
1.	Both Committees decided in future to abolish printed Minute So The place of these Minutes will be taken by a report of a rather more detailed nature than we have had in the past to the Council. There will still be roneod Minutes fcr the record and fox the guidance of tie members of the Committee.
2.	The Child Welfare Committee agreed to adopt the new form of agenda as used for the Council.
3.	The problem on which I would like your advice is the following:
The number of delegates, substitute delegates, assistant delegates and advisers sent to the Committees seems to increase annually, in spite of the fact that when the Traffic in Women and Children Committee was first set up by the Council it was clearly stated that "the countries shall each be invited to appoint a representative", and that the original intention of the Council, as is clearly shorn, was that the Committee should be kept as small as possible.
It was originally decided that the Committee should consist of five or sin Government representatives and cf three or five assessors. The facts to-day are as follows:-
Government representatives .
.assessors
Traffic in Women ana. C h i 1 dr en C omm it te e
12
5
Child Welfare Committee
12
8
This increase of representation ecu Id, I think, not
he avoided.. Add fo these figures, however, the fact that at the recent meetings France had three delegates (2 delegates and 1 technical adviser] to the Traffic in '.¿omen and Children Committee aid four delegates (3 delegates and 1 technical adviser) to the Child Welfare Committee. Spain had two delegates to each Committee. Italy had two delegates (1 delegare and 1 technical adviser) to the Traffic in Women and Children Committee, Sng 1 and had two delegates (both from the same Government department) and Germany 2 delegates for both, making in all twenty-one Government representatives for the Traffic in Women and Children Committee and twenty for the Child Welfare Committee quite irrespective of assessors. Such an increase adds, not only very greatly to the expense of documentation where the distribution of large printed documents is concerned, hut also very much to the length of the debates. The delegates are, therefore, forced either to remain longer in Geneva for their meetings or t o deal hurriedly at the end of the meeting with subjects which still remain on the agenda, (which axe very often of great interest,) because so much time has been devoted to speeches. One occasion occurred whan three members of the same delegation spoke at some length one after the other. The Chairman did not feel it possible to lay down any rule as to only one member of a delegation speaking, except at the formal request of the head, of the delegation, as he feared it would give offence.
5
I appreciate the fact that it might he difficult to ash States to limit their delegations, hut c œ Id not perhaps some general rules of procedure as to speaking etc., he drawn up for League Committees as a whole? I would suggest that these rules should also contain mention of some of the restrictions of the Publications Committee (for example, that
no document exceeding fifty -pages may he roneod). The en-
has
forcement of this ru le,/on more than one occasion, been very much resented by the Rapporteurs who feel that information of real interest has to he omitted without real justification, if the Secretariat are really to enforce such regulations it would he better to have some gene rail;/ approved regulations which can he circulated to all Committee members. I should he very interested to know the experience in other Committees and wonder whether you would consider the possibility of settingup a small Sub-Comm it tee to consider the question, or of at least raising the matter at the Director's meeting.
If such general rules were set up should they not also include mention of the fact that it is not possible to hold meetings at certain periods of the year and general directions as to the latest dates on which documents sent in to the Secretariat for circulation can he received. If the Council could he induced to approve some such general regulations I think the position of Secretaries to League Committees might be made somewhat easier .

ANNEXE 1.
1.	Nomination de trois Membres non permanents du Consei 1.
{Résolution adoptée le 15 septembre 1926)
Chaque année, au cours de la session ordinaire, l'asseiblée procède à lr élection de trois Membres non permanents du Conseil» Ceux-ci sont élus pour une période commençant immédiatement après leur élection et se terminant le jour où l'Assemblée aura procédé aux élections, trois années après.
2.	Convention sur l'esclavage: Rapport annuel du Conseil, (Résolution adoptée le 25 septembre 1926)
LTAssemblée désire que la Société des Nations continue à s'intéresser aux mesures destinées à assurer l'abolition progressive de l'esclavage et des conditions analogues, et elle invite, en conséquence, le Conseil à préparer et à communiquer chaque année à l'Assemblée un document indiquant les lois et règlements que les Parties à la Convention de l'esclavage auront, en vertu de l'article 7, communiqué au Secrétaire général et à y joindre éventuellement les interjetions supplémentaires que les Membres de la Société seraient disposés à fournir spontanément sur les mesures prises par eux à cet effet.

Directors' Meeting Paper 10.1928. Genera, April 20th, 192Q.
COMMITTEE WORK AND THE CENTRAL SERVICES. Memorandum by M. Huston.
M. HUSTON:
Je trouve vos propositions très intéressantes et je propose qu'elles soient ronéographiées et distribuées aux Sous-Seoretaires Generaux, aux -Directeurs et Chefs de Sections de manière qu'elles puissent être
P	#
discutées avec le memorandum de Pâme Rachel Crowày par le
0	é P	P
Comité qui a ete constitue dans ce "but.
(Signe') Paulucei di Calboli Barone.
19.4.1928.
The Secretary-General.
With reference to Directors' Meeting Paper Ho. 8 - "General Rules of Procedure for League Committees"
I should be glad if, when this paper is being considered^ the question of the relation of the committees' work to the central services could also be taken into consideration.
During the last few years, owing chiefly to the rapid increase in the number of committees and conferences held in the Secretariat, the work of the central services has become more and more exacting and it is the rule rather than the exception that attendance beyond the normal working
hours should he required from the members of the stafft
In the first years of the leagueTs existence, it was necessary from time to tine for a special effort to be made in order to facilitate and expedite the work of some of the committees. It was, however, supposed at that time that this state of things was more or less abnormal and that the work of the Secretariat would eventually be organised on normal lines and carried out in normal office hours.
This expectation has not been realised and any efforts which have been made to reduce the pressure of work of the central services have been counterbalanced by the large increase in the number of conferences and conmittees in session during almost the whole year. In consequence, it is frequently necessary for the central services to institute a complete or partial shift system to cepe with the night work involved. This shift must of necessity be composed of only a few persons and it often happens that these few persons are called upon to work until 3 or 4 a.m. because of the manner in which the work is organised,
for example, during the recent meeting of the Committee on Child Welfare, I was asked in the afternoon whether it would be possible for a report to be duplicated that night. The manuscript was promised to me for not later than 8.30 p„m„, but the last of it was not delivered until after 11.30 p.m. When the document was practically-completed, the President requested that a considerable number of changes be made. A protest against this was made, to which the President replied nJe crains que vous ne montriez de la mauvaise volonté”1. This is not very encouraging for the persons who are called upon to work until 2 or 3 a.m, to get the work finished.
~ <3 -
I give this as an example, tut the sane thing occurs in a considerable number of committees« The drafting of reports is left until the very last minute and then it is expected that the document will be rushed in a few hours* The fact that there are a number of committees in which this never happens indicates that it is quite possible to organise the work differently.
Another point which I; should like raised is the question of the preparation of urgent documents for a committee, which have to be duplicated during the night and delivered at the hotels in the early morning. It frequently happens that these documents, for one reason or another, are handed in to the duplicating department only at midnight, when a little foresight would have made it possible for them to be handed in two or three hours earlier.
A third point to be considered is the great rush of work poured in upon the duplicating department one or two days before the meeting of a committee. It often occurs that on Saturday we are given a large number of documents to be reproduced for the following Monday, hence it becomes necessary to keep a staff on duty all day Sunday, This has happened on at least three occasions this year, the work in each case consisting of several hundreds of pages*
4
The fourth point is the length of the minutes of cummittees, It appears to me that there is a very great tendency to make these minutes much longer than has been the oase heretofore. I'or example, Pc.Vo6 of the 24th Session of the Economic Committee consisted of 44 pages in one language. Double this figure for the other language, taking into account that the document must be reduplicated after revision, and it mill readily be seen the vast number of pages which must be run through the machines before the minutes of all the sessions of any of the committees are finished„
I have considered what practical rules might be laid down in order to remedy the situation. I think that if certain rules were laid down and rigidly enforced, it would become necessary - and therefore possible - fox committees1 secretaries to arrange their work to conform to the rules. I would propose
1)	that except curing the Assembly ox other large Conferences, the Secretariat should close down entirely at midnight at the latest and that any work that cannot be completed by then should be left until the following' morning. In order to render this arrangement more practical, arrangements might be made exceptionally for work to begin
at G a.a. in cases of particular urgency,
2)	that all work after 8 p.a., whether on a shift system or not, should be counted as time and-a-half»
There can be"no doubt that for the majority of the staff night work is much more trying iroa every point of view
5
than wot le the ouest: factor, tl irregular ordinary !
X 3 o 4 c & 3 »
acconpfished in normal office hours» Apart ion of physical fatigue, which is in itself a ;s shift system means irregular hours of sleep meal-times and a complete disorganisation of Life .

Chief of Internal Services»

HOTA LY THL SBCKlTAHY-GHULRAL.
I have learned on good authority that it is probable that the Brazilian re pi;; to the Council invitation will be in the negative, though the Brazilian Government will state that it voulu line to continue to co-operate in the Technical Organisations of the League, in The Hague Court, and in the International Labour Organisation, refraining only from participation in the political side of the League1© worA.
Such a reply would raise in an acute form the question as to the right policy which the League should adopt in regard to states withdrawing from the league and wishing to continue to collaborate in certain of the League activ ities*
The matter is of such importance that I should be glad 11 it could be considered by the Under Secretaries General and Lixectors and discussed at the LirectorsT Meeting on the 25th April or on the 2nd May.

LEAGUE OP FAT IOUS

May 2nd, 1928.
LEAGUE COMMITTEE WO EE.
Memorándum ty Dame Rachel CRCWPT.
The following note frora Dame Rachel Crowdy to tha Secretary-Gane ral is circulated t» Under Secretarles-General, Directors and Chisfs of Section and is for consid eration by the Special OomniitteQ appointed at the 3>irectorsT meeting of Anril 18th. (Sir Axthur Saltar, M. Coiban,
M. Catastini, Dame Rachel Crowdy, Mr, Rajchman and M. Haas}.
3ECRETAHY-GBHERAL:
I read with great interest Directors1 Meeting Paper No. 10, which gives Mr. HustonTs comments on my memorandum regarding League Committees.
I agree with much of what Mr. Huston says but, naturally and quite rightly, he has considered the question mainly, if not entirely, fron the point of view of the Central Services. I am anxious that some general rules of procedure should, if possible, be drawn up which will protect the junior personnel within the Sections also. I fear that no further instructions to Secretaries would improve the situation and might only save the Central Services at the expense of Sectional staff.
It is often, if not always, possible in a stress of w*rk for the Pool to be divided, as Mr. Huston says in his memorandum, into shifts; a Section is seldom large enough for any such arrangement to be made. The result is that Secretaries
in Sections are sometimes of necessity called upon to wort
2

twelve hours a day or even more during a Commit tea, and for those Sections which serve several Committees this means a constant and somewhat inhuman stress of work:.
Much of the "rush work" is due to the fact
(a)	that Governments send in re'ports and replies to questionnaires at the very last moment. i*or example, it was necessary for a large part of the Social Section to work throughout the four Saster holidays owing to the fact that Holland and Switzerland sent in their memoranda on "methods of administrative control of the drug traffic " at the very last moment, in spite of the fact that they had had. six months in wh ich to prepare and sutanit their memoranda;
(h) that the dates of meetings are sometimes fixed hy Committees at vary awkward times (l have already gone into this in qy previous memorandum);
(c)	that members arrive at Committees with documents which they wihh circulated immediately to all the members of the Committee. We had an example of this recently when a member of the Committee arrived with something like a dozen communications which he wished translated and circulated immediately*
If rules are merely laid down "for the guidance of Secretaries" we shall, I think, not improve the situation very much* Most Secretaries are in any case on good terns with their Committees and are already out of tbeir experience able to bring a good deal of influence to bear on Presidents and members. 'What is wanted is something more on the Imes of rules of guidance for all League Committees <, These rules would undoubtedly assist Secretaries. If the iules are for Secretaries only and the Secretaries try to enforce them, an element of bad blood might easily enter into the situation and we might al 1 he faced with the type of remark which Mr. Euston quotes in his
memorandum.
With regard to the preparation of reports, I imagine that most Secretaries arrange to write a certain part of the report every day and if the President is young and in sines a-like it is often possible to get him to approve this daily. If, on the other hand, the Chairman is old and tired it is extremely difficult for a Secretary to insist on hie keeping up to date with the report. On a recent occasion two reports which had been prepared daily by us were only considered by the Chairman after the close of the last meeting of the Committee. I think we shall always have to face the fact that it may be necessary to write, translate and circulate the reports of Committees to the Ccxancil at very short notice, the only alternative sometimes being to keep members (who are busy people and are anxious to get back to their Government Department posts), hanging about Geneva for a couple of days. I am sure however, that much could be done to lighten the work of the Sections and of the Central Services if general rules to Committees cculd be approved by the Council or by the Assembly and circulated to the Committees.
I would make one further suggestion and that is that greater care should be taken to see that too many Committees are not allowed to meet during the same month as undoubtedly this alstf affects the situation at present under discussion. If possible Committees should be encouraged to settle the date of their next meeting before adjourning in order that it may be known sometime beforehand what months are free for extraordinary Oommittee meetings and in order that arrangements can be made to cope wit> "rush work" in the event of two or three Committees sitting at the same time.
(Signed) Bach el CBCWDY,
Chief of the Opium Traffic and Social Questions Section.
24»»4— 20.
This should be circulated to Under Secret aries^General, Directors and Chief of Sections and be considered by the Committee which it was decided to set up at the last Directors' meeting.

II - Séances des Commissions.
La recommandation faite par le Bureau les années précédentes au sujet du travail des Commissions devra être renouvelée cette année. Il serait désirable, en effet, que, sauf dans des circonstances exceptionnelles, chaque Commission ne siège qu'une fois par jour, et que, pendant la période de quelques jours qui, selon toute vraisemblance, sera consacrée exclusivement aux séances des Commissions, une séance tenue l'après-midi, ne soit pas ajournée au lendemain matin, mais au lendemain après-midi.

La question N ° 9 "construction des nouveaux immeubles" fit, au cours de la dernière session, l'objet d'un examen préliminaire du Bureau et ce fut, sur la base des propositions du Bureau que le Comité spécial fut désigné par l'Assemblée. Il paraîtrait donc naturel que le Bureau délibérât sur la procédure à suivre au cours de la présente session.
La question N° 21 "Election d'un nouveau Membre de la Cour". Il y a lieu de se référer à la procédure suivie au cours de la 4me Assemblée, appelée à élire le remplaçant de M, Ruy Barboza, décédé.
L'Assemblée fut saisie d'une 1-Tote émanant du Bureau et portant aussi bien sur les dispositions prises en vue de l'élection que sur la procédure et selon laquelle elle aurait lieu. Il serait opportun de se conformer à ce précédent et de renvoyer cette question au Bureau, qui fixera la date à laquelle aura lieu l'élection simultanée par le Conseil et par l'Assemblée, du nouveau Membre de la Cour et arrêtera le texte d'uns Note analogue à celle qui fut communiquée aux Délégations en 1323.
la question N° 24 "Proposition des Gouvernements allemand, britannique et français", son renvoi préalable au Bureau se justifie également par un précédent.

VII.	Dispositions diverse s,
Les Secrétaires des Commissions devront indiquer clairement aussitôt que possible aux Commissions, les documents qui sont mis à leur disposition et qui contiennent des éléments d'information sur les questions qui leur sont soumises. Il est désirable que la marche du travail des Commissions et l'ordre dans lequel les questions diverses seront abordées, soient réglés autant que possible, à l'avance.
Afin d'assurer la coordination des travaux dos différentes Commissions, une réunion des Secrétaires des Commissions aura lieu sur convocation dans le Bureau du Secrétaire général-adjoint avant la séance de l’Assemblée, de façon que le Secrétaire général puisse être tenu quotidiennement au courant du progrès dos discussions et régler en conséquence l'ordre du jour des séances do 1'Assemblée.

As M. Dufour and II» Comert suggest in their minute of the 25th June-, that a Directors' Meeting should be asked to decide the question of precedent which would be created if the Secretariat exhibits at the Barcelona Exhibition, I think the decision had better be taken by such a meeting.
I regret, however, that it has taken so long for the estimates to be prepared,
M. Comert will remember that v/hen he pressed for participation at the Cologne Exhibition I definitely raised the objection that this would raise precedent for exhibiting in other towns, and I received from M„ Comert a most formal assurance that such could not in any way be the case,
: cow find that the fact that we exhibited at Cologne is quoted by a member of the Information Section as a strong reason for exhibiting at Barcelona. I am convinced that we cannot, without laying ourselves open to strong criticism, consent to exhibit at ail these international exhibitions which are now so numéro us.
The only ground cn which am exception might be made in the case of Barcelona is cecause of tne ei^-ecu ubsi, such an exhibition would have in the countries of natm America.
This Minute should be added to Latin American Meeting Papers 17 and 17(a) for discussion at a directorsT meeting.
( Initialled) L. B

YJTiriER HOURS OF SERVICE E.? THE SECAETAEIkT.
The Secretary-General:
Attached hereto you will find a petition signed by a large number of the members of the Internal Services requesting that the office hoars for the winter should be the same as for the summer,namely, from 9 a.m. until 5.30 p.n. with 1-^- hears for lunch.
As I explained to you yesterday in cur conversation, the question of office hours is one which affects the Internal Services much more than the Sections. These Services are able, in general, to observe the definite office hours and the heads of the various Departments are in a position to insist that their staff keep such hour's.
In the Sections this is not always the case as on account of committee meetings the hears of attendance, while in many eases being longer, are much more irregular.
I am of the opinion that to agree to the request contained in this petition will not in any way affect the work of the Secretariat.
-he hours worked will be the sane, i.e. seven per day, Arrangements are always made in all the Internal Services for persons to be on duty after the normal office hours to attend to any urgent work which may arise.
Under these conditions I would urge you to agree to the request and to allow the normal hours for offioe attena.anoe during the winter months to be from 9 a.m. to 5.30 p.m. There would be one further advan-Lage , namely, that 3§- hours would be worked on Saturday. When the office
v
coutb have been from 9.30 a.m. in the winter, it has sometimes been difficult to arrange this, as most of the people who live in Pensions ^dst leave the offioe at 12.30.
October 10th,1928.	(signed) Howard Huston
Chief of Internal Services. I
I will put on Agenda for next Directors’ meeting. Mr.Huston’s Minute can be circulated meantime, Until a decision is reached summer ‘^drs can be maintained.
(signed) E.D.

NOUVELLE METHODE L'INFORMATION POUR LA PRES SE Mémorandum de M. Comert Le Secrétaire Général.
A 1Toccasion de la prochaine réunion de la Conférence de la double imposition et d'évasion fiscale, nous avons eu lridée, à la section d’information, de faire faire, par le Secrétaire de cette Conférence, M. Rueff, de la Section Economique, un exposé devant les journalistes qui viennent habituellement nous voir,
Cette pensée nous est venue à la suite d'un exposé analogue que M, Ruef avait fait hier à la Section d'information et que nous avions jugé fort utile, Il nous est apparu que les Représentants de la presse tireraient un grand avantage à écouter un résumé clair et précis de l'oeuvre de la future Conférence, étant donné notamment le caractère extrêmement technique de ce travail,
Les journalistes sont venus au nombre d'une quarantaine.
La conférence a duré plus d'une heure et a eu un plein succès,
De nombreuses questions ont été posées. L'impression générale a été très satisfaisante.
Au début de la séance, j'avais pris la parole pour dire que i;exposé de M. Rueff ne devait point paraître dans les journaux comme une déclaration du Secrétariat ou comme un interview de la Section Economique, mais que les journalistes devaient le
c+ fJ
/
I
- 2 -
considérer comme une sorte d'information générale, mais a'un caractère un peu confidentiel, qu'ils pourraient utiliser au fur et à mesure du développement de la Conférence.
Cette première expérience paraît avoir donné de si Ions résultats que J'envisage la possibilité de la renouveler dans des cas analogues et, au besoin même, d'instituer deux réunions successives, l'une de langue anglaise et l'autre de langaie française .
Toutefois, comme il ne s'agit plus d'un essai maintenant, mais d'introduire en quelque sorte dans notre travail une nouvelle méthode d'information pour la Presse, Je voudrais, avant de prendre aucune mesure, connaître votre opinion à ce sujet.
(sièfié) Pierre COLIERÎ,
16 octobre 1928.
idea seems to me to be both attractive and good. Let us first 11 discuss it at a Directors Meeting a little later and for purpose circulate,if you agree, your present note.
(s) E.D.
19.10.28.

Confidential circular 1.1908.
NOTE 3Y THE SICHETAP.Y- GENEPAI.
I circulate, herewith, a draft memorandum to the Council on the question of the Yearly number of Council Meetings, etc.
I should he glad to receive any observations on this draft as soon as possible.
(Intlld.) E.D.
17th January, 19 28.
YEA ELY NUMBER ON COUNCIL MEETINGS, ETC.
HEMOEaNDUM 3Y THE SLOELiARY-GSNEBaL.
^t its meeting of Pc comber 9 th, the Council instructed the Secretary-Seneral to submit a further report on the question of the yearly number of its sessions, and on certain questions of its procedure connected therewith.
In regard to the question of the probable effect of the proposed change on tae carrying out of the regular business of the league in its various fields, the Secretary-General thinks that there is nothing to alter in the report which ho had the honour to submit to tho Council in	of last year.
(Document No .....The views therein expressed have
not, so far as he knows, been questioned either in the Council itself or by the members of the different Committees, and no change has taken place in tnc situation since that time.
Certain points were, however, raised in the Council discussion which had not been touened upon in that memorandum.
-s-
fa) There appeared to be general agreement regarding the extreme desirability of the presence of actual Ministers of Foreign iffairs at the Council. Hot only is the work of the Council facilitated through the attendance of Statesmen who arc able to represent more authoritatively the views of those Governments than can usually be done by those who are not actually in charge of their foreign relations, but a valuable occasion is afforded for personal intercourse and for an interchange of views on various problems unconnected with the Council's agenda.
Should the suggested change take place, these occasions, which are usually followed by a period of special tranquility in international affairs will approximately occur once in every 3-g- months instead of, according to the present system, rather more frequently than once in every three.
Various members of the Council have pointed out, however, that attendance at the present number of meetings imposes so great a strain on a Minister of foreign Affairs that, unless sane alteration is made, it is likely to become impossible-for him to continue to attend personally all the Meetings of the Council.
(b) Several Members ox the Council referred to the effect on public opinion, especially in circles particularly favourable to the league, of a reduction in the number of meetings of the Council, and it was suggested that the Secretary-General should inform the Council as to what evidence of anxiety in thi3 connection had reached the Secretariat. The only tangible proof of such feelings of this nature which has been received is a resolution adopted by the International Moderation of League #f nations Societies in the course of its meeting held in
-3-
Berlin in the month of May, 1927.
(c)	In the Council discussion emphasis was laid on
the fact that, under tho new system hy which Don-Permanent
Members of the Council take their seats immediately on election,
the Council now really holds two separate sessions, one of
which begins a few days before the Assembly and continues
until the election, while the other begins with tho first
meeting of the Council in its new composition and continues
until a few days after the close of the Assembly. It was
further pointed out that if, as has not been fully the case
up to tho present, the Council makes it a regular practice to
continue the second of these two sessions for so long a
period after the Assembly as will enablo it to deal with all
■work arising out of the Assembly which does not necessitate
new and protracted study before a Council decision is -possible,
it becomes somewhat unnecessary for it to meet again so soon
as tne first days of December, i.ei, only two months after the
previous meeting. It is, of course, not for the Secretarv-Sen'ral
to give the Council any advice as to what its final decision in
the matter may be, but he may perhaps bo permitted to remark
that should it resolve to substitute meetings in January and
May for its present meetings in December, March and June,
it would perhaps be advisable for the purpose of adequate
transaction of business, that it s mould resolve to embody imj-ts
7T) The text of Wis" resolution is as follows:-The XItii Plenary Congress,
Informed that the Council of the I-sague will consider next month a proposal to reduce the number of its meetings from 1 to o in each year,
Considering that public opinion would interpret such a reduction as a sign of the diminishing importance of the league of Nations and that it would lessen the advantages which have resulted during the past year from the frequent personal contacts of tho men responsible for the peace of Europe.
Urges the eminent statesmen and diplomats on the Council of the league to continue to undertake tne labour of quarterly meetings, since European peace is not vet stable, and,
Ventures to urge that so serious a change should not, in any yca.se, be made unit 1 the session of the next Assembly has enabled the new Members of the Council and representatives of other interested States to be consulted.
regular rules some arrangement, in the sense a hove described, for the disposal of work arising out of the Assembly, and that it should definitely declare that the 'Represen tatires of the Kerbers ox the Council are prepared to some to Genera for the pre-assembly session 6 days bofore the Assembly instead of throe, as at present provided for, provided the agenda for the meeting so requires. The President of the Council, in consultation with the Secretary-General, would decide on the exact date of the meeting.
(d)	Attention was drawn to the effect which a reduction of tne number of sessions must have on the tenure of the Presidency of the Council, and certain proposals were made for the better regulation of this point.
In regard to certain suggestions according to which the change in the Presidency should take place at fixed intervals, •o.g„, every two months, the Secretary-General ventures to submit that such a change in the present procedure would involve serious administrative difficulties. It is the regular practice of the Council to entrust certain business which must necessarily be accomplished between its meetings to its President for, in many cases, to its President and its Rapporteur for the particular question). In such cases and also in the cases - less numerous, indeed, but occurring of necessity from time to time - in which the Secretary-General is compelled to consult the President of the Council on minor questions of Council business, a change in tne Presidency might evidently lead to difficulties. This would, of course, be the case particularly if the change happened to -feke place just at the time when the agenda for the forthcoming meeting is submitted to the President. Pinally, should it chance to coincide with the existence ox a sudden political crisis of a serious nature, the effect might be very serious when it is
-5-
rememberad that the Council has laid important duties on the President in such a contingency.
It is true that if there are onl~r three Presidents of the Council each year, there are bound to be a certain number of Members elected for a throe-yearly period whose representatives will no.rer exorcise the Presidency. This possibility remains even if there are four Presidents each year, but it is reduced to a very small proportion. In this connection the Secretary-General ventures to point out that the practice of retaining as President during tho second session which, cp-incido3 with tho Assembly tho same representative as presided over the previous session, has been due , in the two years in which it has been followed , to exceptional circumstances, and need not necessarily be maintained in the future.
a further proposal which was made during tho Council meeting vas that tho Council should appoint a Vice-President. It was suggested that when the President happened to be a representative of a Permanent Member of the Council. tho Vice-President should, bo the representative of a non-permanent Member and vice-versa. In this connection the Secretary-Geno ral ventures to remind the Members of the Council that up to th*'
present no distinction has ever been
made between the permanent-
ana non-permanent Members as regards the incumbent on Members of the Council. An might be that the Vice-Prosidency should alphabetical order as the Presidency but country standing 8th on the list in alp.h: of with the country standing first. In course, the exact difference between the doubtless be disturbed by the effect of
duties and privileges other possible arrangement follow the same s houId begin with the abetical order, instead this case while, of position of the two would chan 503 in the Council13
-6-
composition, it seems probable that, at any rate for a number of years, the Vice-Presidency would always be exercised b a ropres on dative who had neither quite recently held, nor expected quite soon to hold, the office of President.
Certain Members of tae Council were careful to point out that no change would bo made in the essential character of the procedure which it has recognised and approved in connection with the action of the Council in time of crisis. The immediate duty of summoning a special meeting of the Council under Article 11 has to be exercised by the Secretary-General on his own responsibility, a consultation with the President being a matter of possible convenience but not ox necessity. If, however, circumstances demand that action shall be taken in the name of the Council even before the most urgent mooting is possible, it is evidently desirable that that action should, if possible, be taken by the President, ’thus in the report which the Council approved at .its last session on the methods or regulations which would enable the Council to take such decisions as may be necessary to enforce the obligations of the Council as expeditiously as possible, the following paragraph is included amongst those leading with the possible action of the Council in cases where there is an imminent threat of war:-
"Evon before the Council meets, it is desirable that the Acting President should send telegraphic appeals to the parties to tne dispute to refrain forthwith from any hostile acts. The nature of this appeal will n ecessarily vary with the circumstances of each case.
If, owing to exceptional circumstances, the Saeretar y-Gern'i-al considered tnat the Acting President was not in a-position to act, he right request the ex-president most recently in office who is available to take this stop in the name of the Council '.
It is for the Council to sa^ whether if, in such a case, the Acting President were for ary reason not Immediately available the Secretary-General ought to turn in
-7-
the first place to the Vice-President, should it decide to appoint one, rather than to the previous president. It seams clear in any case from the discussion that the Council desires to maintain the tradition by which the president or his substitute takes the action above described in urgent cases on his own authority and without waiting to consult any other member of the Council,
In cither case, it might perhaps bo desirable that the Council should now definitely lav down, as has not been done up to the present, that the President (and the Vice-President, if appointee), should make arrangements as would enable the Secretary-General to be certain of getting into communication with them at a moment of crisis without delay.
If, for instance, the President intends to be absent from the capital in which he resides, it seems desirable that ho should make such dispositions as would enable the Secretary-General to consult him either by telephone or telegraph without delay, or, gh-ould this be for any reason impossible, that he should before leaving, inform the Secretary-General, and either appoint a personal substitute, or authorise him to address himself, should a crisis arise, to the Vice-President or the previous President as the case may be.
another question which arose during the Council discussion was that of the possibility of so arranging ltd business that tne agenda for one of its annual meetings should consist entirely of those matters of regular business which have been so fully studied beforehand by expert Committees, etc-, that the presence of foreign Ministers or other representatives of the Council whose time is exceptionally occupied, might" not
-8-
be necessary. In regard to this point, the Secretarv-General is bound to observe that, in his experience, there has been, at any rate during recent years , no Council mooting which has not included in its agenda a number of questions of sufficient difficulty and importance to warrant the attendance of the regular represen tatives of the Members of the Council.
It would al73.73 be possible, and is, indeed, to some extent already the practice, to arrange tho agenda of each meeting so that tho first day or two are given over to the transaction of non-contentious business, and tho meetings of Committees on Minorities petitions and the lime. It would seem, therefore,

Clausa to ta inserted in the terms of appointment of Commissioners.
As the result ox the discussion at the Direct or sT Meeting, of December 14th., 1927, I h.are decided that chare the League ie responsible for the appointment of a Commissioner ®r representative who talcas office under formal terms of appointment, whether drawn up by the Seeretary-General or merely approved by the Council, a clause should be inserted specifying the Commissioner1 s or représentâtivsTs obligation to abstain from action which might expose hi a to the suspicion of using his position for his private advantage.
The formula approved after the discussion at the Directors1 meeting is as fcllavs:-
"The (Commissioner, etc., etc.,. .... Monsieur X) may not occupy any post or engage in any transaction or business which might be incompatible with the functions which ha is hereby appointed to exercise".
"La Commis saira (ou Monsieur X) nroccupera aucun poste et s'abstiendra de toute transaction ou entreprise qui pourraient otra incompatibles avec les fonctions que lui confère la présente nomination".
One object of the insertion of this formula in the Commissioner1 s own appointment is to enable him to insist uoon his staff observing the same rule. This explanation may be given te the persons concerned when discussing, the terms of their appointment.

SHAITGrHAI, Inarch 30th 1928.
Dr« William ii. ¿tappard,
Geneva.
he ar Bill,
I have long intended to write you a letter giving you some of my Impressions of the Bar east; hut every minute seems fully occupied, and there is so much to he said that it seems impossible to put it into any ordinary letter.
I have just returned from a trip up the Yangtze river to Hankow, thence due Worth by rail a aayTs journey to Marshal ¿eng Yu-hsiang's headquarters beyond the Yellow river, from which I returned via the lunghai and Tsin-Pu rail3T*acLs to hanking, where I spent 10 days before regaining Shanghai. I believe no foreigner had made this trip since the hanking affair a year ago, and no foreign investigator had even entered Honan Province since Hi ley of the London Times mysteriously disappeared at Chengchow last summer "sunk without trace". However I had no unpleasant experience of.any kind at the hands of the Nationalists. On the contrary I was- everywhere received with uniform courtesy and apparent goodwill. I have gained an insight into the Nationalist movement, which is unobtainable by those who confine their investigations to the Treaty Ports and other places within the range of foreign gunboats.
One matter that has greatly interested me is the Nationalist attitude towards the League of Nations. I have talked with several of their most influential leaders about the League. Their attitude has always been the same - one of general indifference.	They say the League has done
nothing for China in the past, and they expect nothing from
3
it at present. They are inclined to the opinion that it is so constructed that it can not he of much use to Asiatic States in the future.	The northern Militarists in their
desperation might clutch at League intervention as the drowning man at the pro verb ial straw; hut the South would resent intervention now, and in truth it is difficult to see how the League could intervene without doing more harm than good.
This seems a gloomy picture of League prospects in the iar Last; yet I am persuaded that the picture has a brighter side. If the nationalists are victorious in the approaching campaign - and on the whole the chances seem to favour them - their leaders will he eager to utilise every means of enhancing their prestige.	One obvious way to do
this is to pay up the arrears due to the League and then demand a permanent seat on the Council.	I am certain
that the nationalist leaders will never take much interest in the League unless they have a permanent seat, hut I am not prepared to say chat they will retire from the League if they do not secure the coveted seat.
I believe it is desirable to arouse as much interest in the League among the Chinese as is possible under present conditiens. The Nationalist movement possesses great vitality, and while it is problematical how successful it will be under its present leadership the future undoubtedly belongs to the Nationalists. There can be no satisfactory law and order in China under any olcs-fashioned militaristic goverrsnent.	Thereafter the League authorities should
avoid taking any action likely to be more helpful to the Mukden party than to the Nationalists. How much more than this the League can do is debatable. I incline to the view
4
that helpful positive action is possible in the interest of the League, even if comparatively unimportant from the Chinese standpoint. I suggest the appointment of some leading Chinese scholars to the Committee on Intellectual Co-operation.
As specimens of the hind of men who might be considered I may mention the following
1.	Dr. Tsai Yuen-pei, formerly Chanoellor of the University of Peking, and now Minister of Education at Hanking.
2.	Dr. Hu Shih, the founder of the movement for the writing of literature in the colloquial language, formerly Professor of Philosophy at the University of Peking.
3.	J.S. Lee, Geologist, Professor of national University at Peking.
4.	Ping Tze, Zoologist, national South Eastern University, at Hanking.
5.	Y.R. Chao, Philologist (Chinese Phonetics)
Professor at Tsing Hua Go liege at x'eking.
If any action were to be taken along the line I have suggested, it would be most effective if taken promptly.
If and when the Chinese set their house in order, they will become more or less intractable like the Turks. Some graoious recognition in good season will go a long way towards reconciling the Chinese towards the League, and greatly facilitate the handling of more difficult problems when they arise.
(Sgd. ) A. H. Holconbe

Confidential Circular 8.1928
denova
May 4th, 19 28,.
LEAGUE COUMUUICATIOLS TO DISTANT STATE MEMBERS,
10ME BY TEE SECRETARY-GENERAL.
A particular case has lately arisen where a Government Member of the league received a Convention at so late a date that it «3 practically prevented from signing with special advantages, since a time-limit had been given with regard to such a course.
This raises, in a concrete form, the question which was dealt with in my Circular To.18 of 1926, and subsequent discussion at the Directors Meeting of the 25th; and I think it essential that the attention of the Undersecretaries General, Directors and Chiefs of Service should again be called to this point. Sot only South Africa, but also Japan, Dew Zealand, and Australia have made informal complaints that time is not given seriously to consider the proposals of the various Organs of the league, with the consequent uneasiness that some day their interests as Members of the League may suffer damage therefrom. This feeling extends even to the United States, though in the last case, America not being a Member of the league would not feel .’justified in making any formal complaint.
In cases where the Secretariat has any control,
I should be glad, therefore, if this question of sufficient
- 2
time is always considered, and where urgency is absolutely necessary and the subject important, the cable utilised,» In cases where the Secretariat has no such control, I third;, however, that it is highly desirable that the Secretary of the Conference or Committee should call the attention of the President to the importan C/LJ Cai "t L’^dL ched to this matter by the distant Members of tho League.
I personally should welcome the point being raised by any of these distant Members at the Assembly, as it would certainly strengthen our hands in the future.
In the case of distant Members of the league, it is sometimes necessary to persuade public opinion that the LeagueTs activity in European political affairs is to be welcomed rather than grudged. It is consequently all the more necessary that those distant Members should feel that in technical questions they enjoy exactly the same privileges as every other Member,
(In til d, )	3.1).

Confidential Circular 9.1928
DETAILS SUR LES EVENEMENTS DE TSINAN POU.
(La note suivants, sur l’incident sino-japonais de Tsinan-Fou, a ôté rédigée en se basant autant que possible sur les informations officielles provenant de source japonaise. Elle a; par conséquent, (sans avoir la prétention d'être une version impartiale et juste) pour but de dégager des nouvelles sensationnelles et quelque peu tendancieuses celles confirmées par les autorités, et d'en établir la valeur réelle).-
Vers le milieu du mois d’avril, 1’armée nationaliste ayant pris l'offensive contre Pékin, et avançant près de la province de Chantung, la sécurité des résidents japonais dans ce territoire a été gravement troublée. En date du 80 avril, après une déclaration du Ministre des Affaires étrangères, le Japon envoya une troupe de 5.000 soldats en Chine, en vue de protéger les résidents japonais ainsi que leurs interets, qui étaient menacés par suite des récentes circonstances de la guerre civile entre l’armée du nord et l'armée du sud. En attendant l'arrivée de ces soldats, trois compagnies, tirées de la garnison japonaise en Chine, seront envoyées à Tsinan Fou, en vue de prévenir toute éventualité.
Le 5 de ce mois, à 9 h.l/S du matin, le Consul .japonais à Tsinan Fou a rendu visite au Général commandant l'armée sudiste, Tchang Kaï Chek, à qui il a expliqué les motifs de l'expédition de l'armée japonaise en Chine, qui n'avait ¿‘autre but que de protéger ses ressortissants et les intérêts de ces derniers.
2 -
Dès que 1 * ordre et la sécurité seraient rétablis, l'armée japonaise serait retirée et cette expédition ne devait être aucunement considérée comme un acte inamical»
Cependant, au moment du retour du Consul apres cet entretien, vers 10 heures passées, les évènements se précipitèrent» A la nouvelle qu * une maison habitée par une famille japonaise aurait été pillée complètement et qu'un agent de police japonais se trouvait on dager par les soldats chinois pilleurs, une troupe de soldats japonais (environ 30) se précipitèrent à l'endroit, Les Chinois s'enfuirent dans la caserne chinoise, d'où les coups de feu ont commencé contre les soldats japonais qui ripostèrent sans retard» Cette bagarre dura jusque vers midi, malgré tous les efforts faits pour arriver à un règlement pacifique de la situation» Les belligérants décidèrent, vers midi, de donner l'ordre de cesser le combat» Malheureusement; cet ordre ne fut pas écouté dans l'armée chinoise et les désordre s continuèrent dans la ville» Ce n’est que vers six heures du soir qu’on put trouver le moyen de négocier entre les deux armées» Le Consul japonais, suivi du chef da l'Etat-major, put conférer avei; le représentant du général Tchang K aï Cher.» Il fut décidé, après ces pourparlers, d'un commun accord, que: 1°) l'armoe nationaliste du sud devait évacuer la concession japonaise et 2°) l'armée nationaliste du sud devait garantir l'ordre et la sécurité dans la concession japonaise- au moyen de forces de police et l’exécution de cet ordre devait commencer le lendemain matin,
4 mai, à, partir de 6 heures»
3 *•
Au lieu d'obéir à l’ordre formel du Commandant Tchang K aï Cheit, les nationalistes ayant groupé leurs forces tentèrent de nouveau d’envahir la concession» Des coups de feu furent tirés des maisons privées de la ville contre les Japonais sans distinction entre les civils et les soldats et, pour faire face à ces attaques, les Japonais durent organiser une énergique défense» Après de longues heures de combat, qui dura jusque dans 11 après-midi du 5 mai, les âoldats japonais réussirent à maintenir leurs positions et à infliger de lourdes pertes à l'armée ëudiste.
Enfin, les dirigeants des deux armées parvinrent, vers cinq heures de l'après-midi, à une entente en vue de cesser le combat. D'après une information officielle japonaise, il y aurait, du côté japonais, 1S soldats tués, 30 blessés. Quant aux civils, on déplore onze morts, dont deux femmes violentées, et une dizaine de blessés. En outre, une centaine de maisons auraient été pillées.
Du côté chinois, on suppose également la perte de vies humaines et les dégâts auraient été plus graves,
Depuis ce moment, bien que les hostilités aient cessé, un ctat de tension grave régnait entre les deux camps sans pouvoir obtenir l’ordre et la paix dans la ville de Tsinan Fou» Le chemin de fer de Tsin Tao à Tsinan Fou a été déjà détruit à plusieurs endroits, et il était à craindre que, malgré tous les efforts de sauvegarde tentés par la troupe japonaise, les moyens de communication entre Tsinan Fou et Tsin Tao soient exposés à une destruction imminente de la part des soldats nationalistes du sud.
4
'Ters 16 heures,	la 7 de	ce mois,	le colonel	Kuroda,
chef de 1'Etat-Major	de l'a	rmée japo:	'¿ai se , à 1 a	su i t e d'un
entretien qu'il a eu	avec 1	e délégué	cLb !_ * armé<=-	nationaliste

1.	- Punition sévère des dirigeants de l'armée nationaliste responsables des incidents ayant causé des dommages à l'armée ainsi ou'aux résidents japonais.
2.	- Confiscation des armes de l'armée responsable.
3.	- évacuation par les soldats nationalistes de la ligne de chemin de fer Tsin Tao-Tsinan Fou.
4.	- Interdiction de toute propagande anti-japonaise.
5.	- Interdiction d'occupation par l'armée nationaliste des territoires s'étendant sur 20 ri chinois des deux cotés de la ligne du chemin de fer Tsin Tao-Tsinan Fou.
Le Chef de 1'Etat-Major de l'armée japonaise a demandé une réponse dans les douze heures. En dépit de ces négociations la situation n'a pu être améliorée; les soldats chinois en civil (benitai), qui n’ont pas toujours su attendre avec discipline les ordres du Commandement Suprême, se sont emparés de la zone longeant le chemin de fer. "Ce serait une grave erreur de croire qu'à la faveur des circonstances actuelles le J^-.pon veuille s 'engager dans une aventure en Chine. Ce que l'on veut à Tokio, c'est sauvegarder dans toute la mesure du possible les intérêts que l'Empire du Sole il-Devant possède dans le Shantung, assurer la sécurité de la ligne Tsing-Tao à Tsinan-Dou, ne pas laisser porter atteinte aux droits des étrangers tels qu'ils résultent des traités. Il y a là une opération de police commandée par les circonstances, mais qui n'a en aucun cas le caractère d'une intervention politique et qui n'est pas contraire, par conséquent, à l'accord de Washington de 1922, lequel, il faut bien le constater, s'est révélé jusqu’ici singulièrement décevant pour l’autorité des puis-, sanees et la protection de le ors intérêts en Chine. Il n’est pas de gouvernement japonais, lut-il plus impérialiste que celui pré si. dé par le baron Tanaka. qui pourrait songer actuellement, en raison de la situation intérieure et de la situation extérieure, à pousser l’intervention dans le Sharhung au-delà de ce qu’exige réellement la défense des droits et des intérêts de ses ressortissants dans cette partie de la Chine«”

At the time the Japanese troops were withdrawn from Shantung the Japanese Government took occasion to declare that while they had no intention of counteracting any particular party or fraction in connection with disturbances in Ohina.. yet if peace and order in localities containing many Japanese residents were disturbed giving cause for apprehension that safety of said residents might be affected, the Japanese Government would be constrained to tahe such measures of self-protection as might be required« In view of the sudden change of situation in Shantung which has precipitated disturbances threatening to involve region where Japanese reside, the Japanese Government are now compelled, in pursuance of above-mentioned declaration, to dispatch from Japan proper contingent of about 5«000 soldiers to Kiaochow Tsinan Railway gene via Tsingtao for protection of Japanese residents. Pending the arrival of these soldiers three companies drawn from Japanese garrison in China will be sent to 'Tsinan as emergency measure to meet the situation»
It need scarcely be stated that the dispatch of troops by the Japanese Government again to Shantung districts is unavoidable measure of self-protection by no means implying anything line unfriendly intention towards China and her people or interference with military operations of any of the northern

Confidential Circular 10.1928. 1er:ève, le 22 mai 1928.
RAPPORTS A TAC LA PRESSE.
La Section ü1Information a récemment attiré mon attention sur un certain nombre de faits éminemment regrettables : communications inopportunes ou prématurées d'informations ou de documents aux journalistes; divulgations d'informations ou de documents.
Bien que ces divulgations ou indiscrétions ne paraissent pas, après enquête, devoir être toutes imputables à des fonctionnaires du Secrétariat, j'estime qu'elles rendent nécessaire une mise au point des rapports avec la Presse et l'observation, à l'avenir, de règles plus précises destinées à empêcher le retour de tels faits st à éviter que le Secrétariat puisse être accusé de négligence ou de légèreté.
o
o o
Il y a actuellement à Genève une centaine de journalistes représentant des journaux ou agences de presque tous les pays du monde. L'autre part la matière d'information à distribuer à ces journalistes devient de plus en plus variée et de plus en plus abondante.
Depuis huit ans que le Secrétariat fonctionne, ses relations avec la Presse ont été correctes. Durant cette période, aucun cas n'a été signalé, d'appropriation par un journaliste d'informations ou de documents à l'aide de moyens contraires à l'honneur professionnel. Si un pareil cas venait à se produire, le Secrétaire général aviserait aux mesures à prendre, d'accord avec la Section d'information et en consultation avec l'association internationale des Journalistes accrédités auprès de la Société des Dations.
2
La complexité du travail de la Société des Hâtions et io grande affluence des journalistes imposent au Secrétariat des devoirs précis et une méthode rigoureuse dans ses rapports avec la Presse.
Il ne faut pas que 1'opinion puisse s'accréditer selon laquelle le Secrétariat pourrait, à l'occasion, se laisser aller à des indiscrétions„ D'autre part, il est indispensable que l'information à la Presse, sous toutes ses formes, soit centralisée et qu'elle soit placés exclusivement entre les mains de fonctionnaires spécialisés au courant des habitudes et des besoins de la Presse internationale.
Entre les journalistes en résidence à lenève, la concurrence est vive. Le meilleur mopen d'éviter tout excès en ce sens est de donner aux journalistes un sentiment de sécurité. C'est à quoi tend le travail do la Section d'information qui reçoit tous les journalistes indistinctement et qui observe, tant dans la communication des informations que dans la distribution des documents, la double règle de 1'uniformité et de la simultanéité. Toute infraction à cette règle doit avoir des conséquences sérieuses.
D'une part, elle- amène une recrudescence de la compétition entre journalistes, cette compétition pouvant aller jusqu'à prendre la forme de rivalités nationales irritantes. L'autre part, elle compromet gravement le prestige et 1'autorité du Secrétariat qui serait vite accusé de traitement préférentiel. Enfin, elle nuit à la Société des Nations elle-même en provoquant inévitablement la ciise en circulation de nouvelles erronées, incomplètes, tendarv eus es
Or, il est apparu au Comité que j'ai institué, sous la présidence de M* DUPOUR-FEEOlïCE, pour examiner les indiscrétions et divulgations qui ont eu lieu récemment, - qu'une bonne partie de ces indiscrétions ou divulgations pourraient être dues à. des imprudences de langage de la part des fonotionraires du Secrétariat et à leur ignorance du but et des méthodes de travail des journalistes.
3
Les journalistes vont et Tiennent aux différents étages, dans les différentes sections du Secrétariat, avec la plus grande liberté. A la longue, ils connaissent beaucoup de fonctionnaires qui peuvent dans certaines circonstances se laisser aller à leur donner des indications ou des documents leur permettant de faire des télégrammes et de prendre ainsi sur leurs confrères un avantage, soit par le contenu meme de ].r information, soit par la rapidité avec laquelle ils ont pu transmettre cette information. Il arrive assez souvent que les fonctionnaires de la Section dT Information spécialement chargés des rapports avec la Presse, perçoivent, dans leurs conversations avec les journalistes et aux questions que ceux-ci leur posent, lTécho d’autres conversations que ces journalistes ont eues ou d'informations qu’ils ont pu se procurer dans d'autres bureaux du Secrétariat.
Le tout cela, il résulte que les fonctionnaires peuvent être amenés, en toute bonne foi, à commettre des indiscrétions ou des divulgations qu’il importe d’éviter. J’ai, en conséquence, décidé de poser les principes et règles ci-dessous.
Ri) IB LL IA SE CTI PL D ’ INFO PI,La T ION.
La Section d’information esc en principe seule chargée d’informer la Presse, de recevoir les journalistes et de leur distribuer des documents,
C’est à elle, en consultation avec le Secrétaire général et les sections intéressées, qu’il appartient de prendre toutes mesures relatives au moment où une information peut être donnée et a la forme que doit prendre cette communication.
A lravenir, les Directeurs et Chefs de Section ou de Service voudront bien donner, dans le plus bref délai possible, les autorisations nécessaires à la Section d’information pour qu’elle Paisse publier aussitôt qu’il est possible de le faire sans
4
inconvénient, telle ou telle information et distribuer tel ou tel document, LTexpérience montre eu effet que le retard apporté dans la publication facilite les indiscrétions, ou les divulgations. En règle générale la Section d’information peut être autorisée à donner une information ou un document le jour meme où cette information ou ce document est envoyé aux Gouvernements, sauf certaines exceptions telles que les rapports de Comités du Conseil, de certaines commissions consultatives, comme la Commission des ilandats , etc.,,
Le Comité, sous la présidence de M. DUROUR-FEROITCE, pourra se réunir de temps en temps et toutes les fois quTil sera nécessaire, pour examiner éventuellement des cas d'indiscrétion
ou de divulgation.
HE ILE S A OBSERVER.
Informations„- Documents.
Je rappelle que le Circulaire spéciale 18, 19C5 fonctionnaire du Secrétariat seignement non public venu à situation au Secrétariat, sa
Statut du personnel (Annexe 71,
) contient déjà l'interdiction à tout de "communiquer à quiconque un renie ur connaissance du fait de leur uf à titre officiel ou avec l'autori-
sation du Secrétaire général".
En dehors de la Section d'information, aucun fonctionnaire du Secrétariat ne peut donner aux journalistes d'informations ou
de nouvelles proprement dites (arrivée ou envoi d'un document; requête; nomination; démission; délibérations privées ou secrètes du Conseil, de Commissions, etc ....} ni distribuer de documents, sauf en consultation avec la Section d'information.
D'autre part, les fonctionnaires sont invités à observer la plus stricte réserve dans leurs propos, avec des journalistes qu'ils peuvent faire du Secrétariat, soit en ville,
lors des rencontres soit dans les bâtiments
Tout fonctionnaire qui serait convaincu de s''être rendu coupable d'une indiscrétion ou d'une divulgation s'exposerait à se voir appliquer les articles 64 et 35 du Statut du personnel.
o
o o
les Directeurs, Croîs de Section ou de Service sont priés de donner connaissance de la présente circulaire au personnel sons leurs ordres. Cette commun!cation qui pourra se faire de la manière que choisiront les Directeurs, Chefs de Section ou de Service, aura un caractère strictement confidentiel.
DOTE EEIATIYE Â Là DAILY SYttOPSI
A l'occasion de l'examen des cas d'indiscrétions et des divulgations ci-dessus mentionnés, le Comité s'est demandé si la
très large distribution qui est faite à l'intérieur du Secrétariat, de la "Daily Synopsis" n'avait pas été ou ne pourrait pas être la cause de certaines indiscrétions involontaires. J'ai en conséquence décidé de restreindre la circulation de ce document,
la "Daily Synopsis" est actuellement distribuée à cent soixante exemplaires. J'estime que ce chiffre est de beaucoup trop élevé et qu'il suffirait que la "Daily Synopsis" fut. distribuée dans chaque section à un très petit nombre d'exemplaires qui seront,
à l'avenir, numérotés.
J'invite en conséquence les Directeurs, Chefs de Section ou de Service, à me soumettre le nombre d'exemplaires dont ils estimeraient avoir besoin pour leur Section et à dresser, à cet effet, une liste nominative aussi restreinte que possible.
^nève, 22 mai 1928.

RECORD OF INTERVIEW :
M.OLIVIERA FERREIRA., the Portuguese Minister at Berne, and their representative on the League, came to see me this afternoon.
He said that they had a comparatively new set of delegates of considerable importance. The Delegation itself would be headed by K.Vasconoellos. Portugal would not this year put forward its candidature for the Council. They felt that they could not do so in view of the probable electiin cf Spain. The fact, however, that this candidature had been put forward fcr the last three or four years without any suocess, and that the loan negotiations had not been successful, had led to considerable criticism #f the League in Portugal. People were inclined to hold that Portugal obtained nothing from the League. He therefore very much hoped that the chief of the Portuguese Delegation might obtain some post at the Assembly which would enable him to take part in the Bureau and made a favourable impression in Portugal.
I replied that I feared that this might be somewhat difficult as within the last three or four years Portugal had already received the Presidency of the Assembly, and also the Presidency of a Commission. In fact, I rather thought that Monsieur Vasoon-cellos had already been on the Bureau.
Monsieur Oliviera acknowledged this, but said that Portuguese history made it right that she should have rather special treatment, and he again pleaded the necessity of something being done to satisfy Portuguese opinion.
I promised him to take note of his request.
(signed) E.D.
August 24th, 1928.

Confidential Circular 12.19 28.
27th August 1928.
M. FOTITCH came to see me this morning. He sail that he had a message from M. Marinkovitch, saying that he was unwilling to accept the Presidency of any of the important Commissions at the Assembly. M. Fotitch wondered whether it would be possible to obtain a Vice-Presidency of the Assembly for M. Ma rink ov i tc h.
I said to M. Fotitch, who fully understood the difficulties, that the competition for these pierces was very great. Usually the chief delegates of the Great Powers were elected by the Assembly as Vice-Presidents, and if this happened there was only one place left, unless the representative of a Great Power became che Président of the Commission. The Prime Minister of Canada was most anxious to be a Vice President, and there was equally La tin-America to consider. Therefore it might be most difficult to comply with M. Marinkovitch* 1s desire.
M. Fotitch then said that M. Marinkovitch had not yet answered his suggestion about the Presidency of the VIth Commission. He would explain that this Commission had the smallest number of questions on its agenda, and therefore would not entail very hard work. He hoped that in these circumstances M. Marinkovitch might accept, but he would let me know definitely during the course of the present week,

M. Guerrero cacao to see me this afternoon.
He ashed me whether I remembered that he had been entrusted last year by the Fourth Committee, acting through a subcommittee, with negotiations with the Government of Honduras for the settlement of their arrears and current contribution.
He had discussed this matter with the Minister for Foreign Affairs of Honduras both at Salvador and Havana. He had explained the scheme approved by the Sub-Committee, and the Minister for Foreign Affairs informed him a short time ago that he was prepared to accept these proposals. He asked, however, that they night be confirmed by the Secretary-General. M. Guerrero had replied that such confirmation was unnecessary since he was acting under the authority of the Assembly.
He had now reccivc-a from the Government of Honduras a cheque for the amount due under the arrangement for 1928. He handed me the cheque and said that ho would let me have a formal note' to-morrow explaining what he had done and confirming the agreement. He would be grateful, if when I received his note, I would telegraph to the Government of Honduras expressing satisfaction and thereby confirming the arrangement.
I said that I would certainly see that this was done.
H. Guerrero added that he would also telegraph to the Government oi Honduras expressing the hope that they would now be represented at the Assembly.	He would suggest that

PEOCBD OF COHYBRSATIOP .
II. Guerrero toll me that he had conversations with II. Pani, the Mexican Minister in Paris. M. Pani sympathised greatly with his, M. Guerrero1 s, endeavours to bring Mexico into the League. M. Pani had said to him that the Government of Mexico was well disposed towards the league cut hardly felt it possible, in view of the question of prestige, to apply for membership without some invitation to do so being addressed to it.
M. Guerrero reminded II. Pani of the demarche made some years ago by the Latin-American countries Members of the League. M. Pani replied that this could not be considered as sufficient since what was required was some measure indicating the- will of the Assembly as a whole. II. Guerrero had answered that he thought such a demonstration might be possible provided that the Assembly were sure that Mexico would give a favourable response to it. Otherwise the League could not take the risk of making ary proposal which might expose it to a refusal or rebuff.
M. Pani had said that he quite understood this and it was agreed between him and M. Guerrero that II. Pani should telegraph to his Government asking whether, if an invitation or expression of opinion were to be sent to Mexico by the Assembly, the Assembly could be sure of a favourable reply.
I said to M. Guerrero that I /as very grateful for the action he had taken and I thought that if there were a certainty of success, the Assembly would not refuse to take an initiative such as he had outlined.

INVITATIONS mm GOVERNMENTS TO UNEERSE C-^í ARIES GEfTRiiT'ANL HE .AS OF IRpIRL^!ITTS
Note 1tj the Secretary-General,
My attention Las teen called to a practice which is obtaining among certain Governments of addressing invitations to Under-Secretaries General and Heads of Departments to pay a visit to their country,
In theory - it would certainly be convenient in practice if the theory were followed - all such invitations ought to pass through the Secretary-General. As, however, it is not practicable to enforce such a rulo on Governments, I should be glad if, when an Under-Secretary General cr Head of Department receives such an invitation, I could be immediately informed thereof, and always consulted before a reply is sent,

Confidential Circular 16.192Qc
Gen ev a,
Octobsr 18th, 1928.
PHRASING OF L3AGP3 DOCUMENTS.
Memorandum 'ey the Se ere tar/•■General.
My attention has been called to certain cases in cLo cu ments ure pared or published by the Secretariat where the use of a particular word or the turn of a ohrase has given offence to Governmental or public opinion in the country or countries particularly concerned. In some cases such offence might be avoided without any prejudice whatever to the substance of the matter by the employment of a synonym or change of sentence. This being so, I venture to remind Heads of Sections that it is always within their competence to ask a national of the country particularly affected by the report or publication to advise on such points. It is, of course, understood that the Head of the Sedtion is in no way bound to ask; for such advice and if he seeks it is at liberty to accent it or not, but perhaps the advantage of having as ou r colleagues nationals of so many different countries is not at present utilised to its full extent.
(Initialled) 3.D
