Geneva, October 18th, 1928c
Vy dear Minister,
Very many thanks for your letters of the 8th, and 9th,
October.
r.eed I say how much I appreciate your known attachment to the ideals of the League and recognise the work which you have done and are doing in order to promote relations between China and the different organs of the League. I was grateful to you for the manner in which you welcomed my suggestion that a mission from the Secretariat should go to China for the above purpose, and of your support of the proposal with your Government. Now that you have received the expected telegram from Nanking we are happily in a position to discuss the various details of the mission, dates, etc. Your suggestion of a meeting between yourself and M. Avenol is, I think, admirable. He is on leave at the present time, but I will inform him of your proposal, and, he will certainly take advantage of it.
I am naturally very anxious that II. Avenol should, if possible, be present at any meeting cf the Council which may take place, and therefore I think that it might be best if it were convenient to your Government that he should leave immediately after a Council meeting in order thet he may, if possible, return in tine for the following one» Of course, I cannot say whether such a course is feasible, but it is one of the peints for further discussion.
The object of the mission is, as stated in my telegram to the Chinese Government, to promote closer relations between the Chinese Government and the organs of the League. The aim of the mission is, therefore, the establishment cf direct contact between
4
the Secretariat and the Chinese Government, in accordance with the decisions taken by the assembly of 1921, through the Deputy Secretary-general .who is eminently qualified to explain fully to the Chinese Government the various activities and organisation of the League,
Believe me, my dear Finister,
Yours very sincerely,

The overwhelming election of Mr. Hoover and. the unexpectedly large accessions of strength jn Congress assuring a single-party control for the first time in years wou id seem to indicate a change in "both the forcefu 1 Iness and the direction Cf American foreign policy within the next few years.
Mr,.Hoobar hrings to the Presidency a now type of man, with new methods of ’work and new lines of interest. In "brief, the engineer end the man of science succeeds, the life-long man of politics, Mr. Coolidgs, end "before him Mr. Harding.

First, and personally, he has had probably the widest administrative experience of any man in high office in the world to-day. Eg had years of engineering work, before the war, in Australia, hew Zealand, Malaya, India, Burma, Ceylon, ¿gypt, °outh Africa end China; he saw war as did few others in his provisioning of Belgium and Germany; he was an important actor in the development of the present international situation at the Peace Conference;, and since then, for eight years as Secretary of Commerce, has had unparalleled contact with AmericaTs whole economic system, especially abroad, through his world-wide system of commercial attaches.
3
Second, and politically* his domestic situation is far tetter than in the herding and Cooiidge administrations• First, public opinion has had time to settle clown after the 1920 upheaval, and is willing to give the President greater freedom of action. Secondly* his party has a tremendous majority in the House and a good majority in the Senate, with 57 Republicans against 39 democrats trad the troublesome Republican radical group practically scat texed „
The first result of this wou Id seem to be a greater forcefulness in the statement of American foreign policy»
As the new government settles into office in the ooming months* it may he expected that the profound upheaval created in Americans rai .t ion ship to the world by an 'snexpacted war and its still more unexpected consequences will finally subside» The United States seems, indeed, to be approaching another phase in the evolution of its world status which may best be forecast, perhaps, as the continual progression of the past» 3>uring the Peace Conference, she enthusiastically espoused a world leader^ ship; immediately after, in a turmoil of uncertainty, she rejected her whole programme and withdraw to a bewildered isolation; soon, however, her more positive elements engendered a humanistic, even if vague and somewhat unresponsible benevolence towards the rest of the world* more recently, the pressure of economic development has necessitated a much more definite and precise statement of her views and desires. That tendency wa may expect to accentuate because both of Anerica7s need for foreign trade and Mr. Hoo7erTs own character end temperament. In other words, the days of sentimental benevolence, ineffective "observers", and informal representation, which alone were possible in the post-war confusion, would seem to have passed in favour of a
4
straight-forward, direct, arid even perhaps at times hardhitting policy of frankness* "We cannot”, said Mr. Hoover in his most important speech accepting the Presidential nomination, "isolate ourselves from the world"»
Similarly, as regards the direction of policy, there is likely to he a change, with a far greater accentuation on the economic side» In the first years after the Peace Conference the only co-operation possible was humanitarian»
Than slowly developed a period of technical co-operation t^end cautious experiments with 17observers"„ During the past year, however, this has widened, especially into the economic field«,
With American industrial expansion increasing e nor mob sly, with
t
American agriculture clamouring for action towards the outside,
¿ust
and with Mr. Hoover having /spent his youth in business in many continents and having completed 8 years of control of the Department of Commerce in Washington, the general orientation of the government seems certain to be profoundly affected»
In a general way, this long experience may be expected permeate Mr. Eoovor?s approach to all the great foreign problems before him. For the Naval Bill,which is probably Congress1 first problem, one may look for strong support for parity, both by personal conviction and political necessity, but opposition,' if only on economic grounds, to the larger programme, for the Kellogg Pact, which is expected to follow, cordial endorsement and support, though not the fervent enthusiasm of a man more given to general psychological statement; for governmental finances, an unwillingness to reduce Allied debts or intermingle with reparations but continuous support of expert settlement of the later, with American financial co-operation; end for world commerce,' great activity, with a high tariff, an attempt to find outlets abroad, especially for agriculture, and an especial orientation towards Latin-America»
As regards ihe League of Nations, Lir« Hoover is likely to bring little change lin fundamental relationship hut a considerable accentuation in co-operation. In the early days a League supporter, he has of recent years node no public statement on the subject until his most important Acceptance speech, wherein, though reaffirming his partyTs present doctrine, he went further than any important American official has yet gone when he said;
.....The United States has refused membership in
the League of Nations but we are glad to co-operate with the League rs endeavours to promote scientific, economic, and social welfare and secure limitation cf armaments".
Obviously, this permits a large field of co-operation® Taken in consideration with other activities already accepted, such as the world Court fcr international law codification, it sets no rigid limit, short of actual membership, to American participation. Almost anything could be included under it, exes ot, perhaps, ’’political'' questions, and no one quite knows where they begin and where they end. If carried completely into execution, it might result in American participation in all league conferences and committees where American attendance was desirable fran the viewpoint either of the United States or of the League. What it leaves uncertain, however, is Amaricars relationship to the two most vital agencies cf League work, the Assembly and the Council*
The exact possibilities of this policy,are, fortunate ly, predictable from past events, so that it is not necessary tc rely only on theory or deduction. Briefly, of all branches of the United States government, except of course, the State Department,
it is with the Department of Commerce, over which Mr. Hoover has presided for 8 years, that the IeagueTs relations have bean the most far-reaching. The details are worth noting as possible indications for the next 4 years;

In other words, Mr. Hoover seems to have acted on the policy that America's own interests are served by co-operation in the league’s economic work; the various branches of his Department of Commerce in Washington have been in helpful contact with Geneva; and the American Delegations to Geneva have included many of his intimate friends and associates, including the heads of six bureaux of the Department of Commerce: Foreign and Domestic Commerce, Research, Regional, Finance and Investment,
Transportation, and Taxation, and numerous Commercial attaches t
There is no reason to think: that co-operation thus freely given in one field should not be extended, as indeed forecast in Mr, Sooverrs Acceptance speech, to other fields which will now cone under his direc-tiona The League, insofar as it is an international conference centre, is a world-wide extension of that practise which has made Mr, HooverTs wort in the Department of Commerce so brilliantly successful; its technical, scientific, co-operative approach to problems follows exactly his own methods, even though its unavoidable slowness and certain fundamental commitments in its organic lav; keep open a tern ramental difference „ But in the day-to-day work, there is much to be gained by both sides from co-operation; the League, indeed, haying invaluable assets, especially in those new fields to which Mr, Hoover both by character and by necessity wj.l'L probably turns international commerce, economics, world agriculture, aviation, and radio,

I saw Harr VON SCEL'BfEf this morning, and ha immediately Broached the possibility of having tha next Meeting of the Council elsewhere than at Geneva«
I told him that then3 were very real difficulties and objections to this. I first of all made the point about the dress and the dissatisfaction which would be caused in Geneva, since we had recently made definite arrangements for new buildings and the Council Hall had been enlarged at Herr StresemannTs own request. People would not understand why, after all this being dene, a Meeting of the Council should be held elsewhere. There were also difficulties with regard to Committees, because we ought to have meetings of the Financial Committee, the Supervisory Committee, and a snail Committee of the Council to deal with the names of candidates for the Opium Central Board.; and, lastly, there was a meeting in Geneva on the 5th December for the Private Manufacture of aips. It might also be said that tha climate of luganc was not much better than that of Geneva, and that there was no reason to gc to the former. Naturally, I was also troubled as regards expenditure, because, although there was a certain amount available in the Budget, it was not sufficient to allow for the hions of buildings for the work of the Secretariat and possibly for the Members of tha Council. In any case, two conditions seemed to me to be essential, the first bo obtain M. Motta’s consent, since the Meeting, if at Lugano, would be held on Swiss soil; and the

second, that an official demand should he made by the Sermon Government. To my mind, it would be much better for the demarche to be made by Eerr Stresemann alone, and not by a small group of Powers, I submitted all these objections; but, if it really was the fact that Herr Stresemann could not come to Geneva, I considered that his presence at the Council Meeting was so essential that the difficulties must not be regarded as a definite obstacle,
Eerr Von Schubert- replied that it was clear that the doctors would not allow Eerr Stresemann to cone to Geneva, but they would allow him to go to I^rgano« He was most snsziou s to cane to the Council, and therefore ha was glad to find the attitude which I adopted* As regards the meeting place of •Ldgancf there was a am all Parliament House at Pugano, which he hoped might be available.
After this short discussion, I sat down with Herr von Schubert, and drafted a telegram which I thought might appropriately be sent, if Eerr Stresemann agreed. This was shewn later to Herr Stresemann, who agreed to its terms.
